Hello Everyone, welcome to CITS2002 Systems Programming in 2017.

FIRSTLY, if you read nothing else, please read this section.

Our unit this year has a record enrolment - about 310 confirmed
enrolments, up from 240 last year.  NOT ALL ENROLLED STUDENTS WILL
FIT IN OUR LECTURES and workshop venues (holding 250-260 students),
and we don't have sufficient lab times or spaces for all students
to be guaranteed a seat in our weekly lab sessions.

IF YOU BELIEVE that you can learn introductory material just as
effectively from lecture recordings, then THIS FIRST WEEK'S LECTURES
may be a good time to put that into practice.  Hopefully we can
manage this way until lecture attendance drops off, as it always
does, in week-2.

*** If you have a specific question that you'd like me to answer in
    the 1st lecture, please just email it to me beforehand.

(I'm not panicking about this and I don't think that you'll need to
 either.  (Too many) years of experience have shown that a reasonable
 proportion of students choose to not attend all lectures and a
 weekly lab, choosing to just watch the LCS lecture recordings and
 use their own labtops for all laboratory and project work.  In
 previous years I have worried about this, adding extra repeat
 lectures, only to find that the only available times and venues
 were at 5PM or 6PM;  barely 20 students attended the repeat sessions,
 and students drifted away from the original lectures anyway.)

-------

We'll spend some time in the first lecture on Tuesday discussing
how the unit will run this year, but first I'll head off some common
questions that students are asking by email:

Firstly, we're not using LMS/Blackboard for this unit.
All teaching materials will be available from:

    http://teaching.csse.uwa.edu.au/units/CITS2002/

LECTURES:
    We have 2 45 minute lectures each week. All lectures will be recorded
    and available via LCS. The schedule of lecture topics, in fact all
    sessions in the unit, is available from the left-margin of the
    unit's homepage. HTML and PDFs of all lectures will be available
    to read or download ahead of time.
    Attendance at lectures is not compulsory.

WORKSHOPS:
    Most weeks we'll hold a workshop on Fridays, to review the week's
    material by focusing on the design and implementation of a short
    program addressing a single problem. You'll receive the problem
    ahead of time, hopefully sketch out a solution, and we'll discuss
    it in the Friday workshops. The workshops are in a lecture venue,
    not a computing lab, so you may wish to bring your laptops along.
    Attendance at workshops is not compulsory.

LABORATORIES:
    The allocated laboratory times are times at which there'll be someone
    there to assist you.  Most weeks, when the two projects are "not
    running", will have a laboratory sheet of 3-4 "standard" questions,
    and often a couple of more difficult questions, for you to attempt
    that week.  Sample solutions to the standard questions will be
    provided.  If you can successfully complete the sample questions,
    each week, then you're keeping up in the unit.
    Attendance at laboratories is not compulsory.  Come along if you
    need to use a computer, or if you need some help with a question.
    There's no requirement to stay the full 2 hours.  Labs are open
    24/7 although, of course, students allocated to other units' lab
    sessions have priority.

Date: Fri Aug  4 04:12:20 2017
To: help2002@csse.uwa.edu.au

Subject: C Naming Convention Question
From: chris.mcdonald@uwa.edu.au
X-smilie: none
X-img: none

Someone emailed this question to me, but the question and reply deserve a broader audience:

____
Hi Chris

I have a question about C naming conventions.

I know from past java courses that functions and variables have a specific convention to 
how they should be represented.

I was wondering what the convention is for this unit?
_____

In CITS2002 I'll not mandate a specific naming/coding convention, and you'll not be 
assessed against one.  Instead we'll identify a few different approaches or techniques when 
first seeing something new.  The primary message is to be *consistent* - whatever 
convention that *you* adopt, personally, needs to be applied consistently through a C 
program, ideally through all of your C programs.  The two projects will be assessed for 
their consistent presentation, but not for a specific one.

I'm not a fan a mandating a single coding convention as I believe it can be resented by 
students that may not like the convention I choose.  Moreover, when you start to read code 
from others, often found on the web, there can be a small 'mental block' in understanding 
that foreign code if it's not a style you're incorrectly expecting, and that can creep over 
into what you understand about a language.

From: poster013@student.uwa.edu.au

Hmm, looks like that safety is refusing to be turned off. I think some (or all) of the lab 
environments have -Wall enabled as a default option (Can not confirm, but I've seen this happen 
before).

To get the effect this exercise is looking for, there may be a compiler option to disable 
warnings.
IMPORTANT: Do Not force-disable all warnings unless you 200% know what you are doing.

With that said, check the manual ('man cc' in the terminal should bring it up). There's a lot of 
interesting stuff in there.

If all else fails, rock up to a lab this upcoming week, the first one is on Tuesday at 15:00 in 
2.03.

The takeaway from this exercise, however, is to always use -Wall and -Werror when compiling. 
Otherwise the compiler will produce a program that will do exactly what you told it to do. Which 
probably wasn't what you wanted it to do. And without -Wall or equivalent, it won't even tell you about 
it.

Previously, <poster083@student.uwa.edu.au> wrote:

> (First time coding c in this unit, remoting into the CSSE computers with PuTTY)

Great to hear that you're making the anticipated progress (tell others that it *is* possible!)


> The program makes a blank line whenever it reads a character that's not between 
> ASCII 48 and 126 (i.e. it shouldn't print that whole 'from original' spiel unless ▒ 
> is between those bounds) 

This certainly sounds like where your error is - at the end of your line containing'hello 
world' will be a newline character;  as it's not an alphabetic character, it shouldn't be 
rotated.

Also, your mentioning that the values needing to be between 48 and 126 suggests that you're a 
bit confused about that bit. We're only rotating alphabetic characters, and the *lowercase* 
alphabetic characters are between decimal 97 (a) and 122 (z) - run   'man ascii'.

Ideally, you should be comparing values against their numeric/integer representations, but 
between the relevant characters - between 'a' and 'z'.

As Trent has stated, sample solutions will be provided at the end of each labsheet's week.  They 
will be *sample* solutions, that answer the question, but shouldn't be taken as the only possible 
solution.

In addition, take care with thoughts such as "Obviously if your code works, it works"; it's not 
always the case.  Code that works for one type or set of input values may not work for all inputs 
(valid and invalid) if it hasn't been widely tested.  And testing only identifies the presence of 
bugs, not the absence of bugs.

Another comment and question sent directly to me via email, that I believe all students 
should read and see my reply:

____
>    I have had some continued trouble with using MacOS during our
>    CITS2002 unit, and others around me have expressed concern too.  I
>    can already see that i will have a significant disadvantage, because
>    im not using MacOS. All the command arguments that you have given
>    us, arent for windows users. The content your teaching us seems to
>    be based around terminal commands in Macintosh. I can already see
>    it will be used more than often in the future.

Your concerns are not unusual and expressed by students, each year,
at the beginning of CITS2002.  Even if you've never used a Macintosh
before, you'll not be disadvantaged relative to other students in
the unit - in fact most students haven't used a Macintosh before,
perhaps other than for some recreational computing.  You're not
being placed at a disadvantage, but are just feeling that you're
outside of your comfort zone.  But you're at university to learn
things, not to keep within familiar bounds.

It's a strong belief within our School and our degree accreditation
bodies that computing students should see a diversity of operating
system platforms and programming languages across their degree, not
just the ones that they already know, so we support and use the 3
primary desktop and server operating systems in our School by
requiring you to use them in different units.  You do not need to
purchase Apple hardware to undertake the unit, can undertake all
laboratory and project work on Linux instead (or, as well), and can
access the Macs in Lab 2.03 from across the network, without needing
to sit in front of one.

>    Would you please add material for windows users, as the majority
>    of us im sure are using this platform?, and im sure a majority in
>    the workplaces are aswell. I cant really afford to buy any apple
>    hardware, so even if you were to take advantage of Linux's free
>    open source OS, that would be a help too im sure.

While it's certainly true that the Windows operating system dominates
the desktop environment, and thus most people are more familiar
with it, there is a significant proportion of the computing world
that isn't dominated by Windows - and that's the back-end server
market, the cloud computing market, and the embedded systems market.
The great majority of computing in these 3 areas is performed by
open-source Unix-based systems, such as Linux and BSD-based systems.
To the extent that we're learning about open-source software,
interfaces, and libraries in CITS2002, the Mac operating system
provides strong (often the strongest) support for the standards
that we're learning about.

Hi Sam,

Glad that you've found the problem.
Also, I'm a pedant when it comes to correct terminology, so let's use "code" or "program", in preference to  
"script", as we're not interpreting the C code.
 
> Also fixed, I had it include 65-90 as well as 97-122 because of the uppercase functionality part.

Good, but it's much preferred if you make your comparisons between 'A' and 'Z', and 'a' and 'z', rather than 
their numeric equivalents - the code becomes far more readable.

Even, even, better is to use some standard C functions,  declared in the <ctype.h> header file, which are named 
isupper() and islower().

... is now available from:

   http://undergraduate.csse.uwa.edu.au/units/CITS2002/workshops/mycal.c

The provided solution is very close to the one we developed in Workshop-2, with comments added so
we'll still understand it in a few weeks' time.

As presented, this solution is "at the level" of understanding that I'm hoping/anticipating that
all students will now have at the end of Week-2.  The solution doesn't use anything complex, just
concepts that have been introduced in lectures and Labsheet-1.

--------
OK, how can we improve it?
The solution's biggest flaw is that we must edit and recompile it to make it work for another month
and year.  Instead, we prefer the program to accept the required month and year via the command-line.
To start, we'll also need to provide the weekday of the 1st of the month, too.

Modify the program (work out where!) to call C's atoi() function, which will convert a character
string to an integer:

  if(argc == 4)
  {
     month    = atoi( argv[1] );
     year     = atoi( argv[2] );
     day1     = atoi( argv[3] );
  }

and now run the program as:    ./mycal 8 2017 5

--------
But knowing and having to provide the weekday of the 1st of the month is annoying, so we ask (more
complicated, but still standard) functions to provide this information.
A commented version of this code is available from:

  http://undergraduate.csse.uwa.edu.au/units/CITS2002/workshops/first_day_of_month.c

** WARNING - beyond the anticipated knowledge of most students at this stage:

Provide additional code:

#include <string.h>
#include <time.h>

int first_day_of_month(int m, int y)
{
    struct tm   timeptr;

    memset(&timeptr, 0, sizeof(timeptr));
    timeptr.tm_mday     = 1;
    timeptr.tm_mon      = m-1;
    timeptr.tm_year     = y-1900;
    mktime(&timeptr);

    return timeptr.tm_wday;
}

and call it with     day1 = first_day_of_month(month, year);

and now we only need to run our program with:   ./mycal 8 2017

Subject: Labsheet 1 sample solutions now available
From: poster054@student.uwa.edu.au
X-smilie: none
X-img: none

Previously, <chris.mcdonald@uwa.edu.au> wrote:

> They're now available via our Schedule page  (though just the basic, non-chili questions).

> If you have a solution to any of the chilli questions, please free to display them here.

This is my solution to questions 6 and 7. Question 6 is handled through the extra loop in the
else statement in main(). Question 7 is embodied in changes to the way ROT is initialised and
passed through the functions (passed to rotate() in main()). If anyone has feedback on a better
way to integrate ROT and pass it to the rotate function I would be interested.

// rot.c

#include <stdio.h>
#include <stdlib.h>
#include <string.h>
#include <ctype.h>

// Compile this program with:
//      cc -std=c99 -Wall -Werror -pedantic -o rot rot.c

//  The rotate function returns the character ROT positions further along the
//  alphabetic character sequence from c, or c if c is not lower-case

char rotate(int ROT, char c)
{
        // Check if c is lower-case or not
        if (islower(c))
        {
                // The ciphered character is ROT positions beyond c,
                // allowing for wrap-around
                return ('a' + (c - 'a' + ROT) % 26);
        }
	else if (isupper(c)) {
		return ('A' + (c - 'A' + ROT) % 26);
	}
        else
        {
                return c;
        }
}

//  Execution of the whole program begins at the main function

int main(int argc, char *argv[])
{
	int ROT = atoi(argv[1]);
        // Exit with an error if the number of arguments (including
        // the name of the executable) is not precisely 2
        if(argc < 3)
        {
                fprintf(stderr, "%s: program expected more than 1 argument, but instead
received %d\n", argv[0], argc-1);
                exit(EXIT_FAILURE);
        }
        else
        {
                // Calculate the length of the first argument
                //int length = strlen(argv[1]);

                // Loop for every character in the text
		for(int k = 2 ; k < argc ; k++)
		{
                	for(int i = 0; i < strlen(argv[k]); i++)
                	{
                        	// Determine and print the ciphered character
                        	printf("ciphered: %c original: %c position: %i \n",
rotate(ROT,argv[k][i]), argv[k][i], i);
                	}
		}

                // Print one final new-line character
                printf("\n");

                // Exit indicating success
                exit(EXIT_SUCCESS);
        }
        return 0;
}

From: poster026@student.uwa.edu.au

Nice work Jessica. It looks like I've implemented mine essentially the same way you have. I managed
to rig it up so that it can read files as well although I'm sure there is a simpler way to do it
than I have... I attempted to redirect stdin to read the file but then had trouble with the
argument count and vector arguments for the main function. 

If anybody is offended by my code (haha!) I'd appreciate some feedback so that I may write better
code. Cheers!

Anyway here it is:


#include <stdio.h>
#include <stdlib.h>
#include <string.h>
#include <ctype.h>

/********************************************************************
 * Program: cipher alphabetic input strings.			    *
 *								    *
 * Usage: enter the program name followed by int, string, 	    *
 * and/or file name arguments (seperated by spaces) ie;             *
 * prompt> ./rot 13 hello text_file.txt 	 	  	    *
 *				 				    *
 * Output: columns representing the position of the character,	    *
 * the unciphered character and the ciphered character respectively.*
 *******************************************************************/								    
 
char rotate(int rotation, char c)
{
	// Check if c is lower-case or not 
	if (islower(c))
	{
		// The ciphered character is ROT positions beyond c, 
		// allowing for wrap-around
		return ('a' + (c - 'a' + rotation) % 26);
	}
	else if (isupper(c))
	{
		return ('A' + (c - 'A' + rotation) % 26);
	}
	else 
	{
		return c;
	}
}
	
void processFile(FILE *fp)
{
	// Initial rotation
	int rotby = 13;
	// To hold scanned in strings
	char ch[1000];
	
	// Scan in strings from file
	while (fscanf(fp, "%s", ch) == 1)
	{
		// Check if first char is digit
		// If so set new rotation
		if (isdigit(ch[0]))
		{
			rotby = atoi(&ch[0]);
		}
		// Cipher each char of the string and
		// print results to stdout
		else 
		{
			for (int i = 0; ch[i] != 0; i++)
			{
				printf("%d  %c  %c\n", i, ch[i], rotate(rotby, ch[i]));
			}
		}
		printf("\n");
	}
}
	
int main(int argc, char *argv[])
{
	if (argc < 2)
	{
		printf("usage: enter program name followed by int, string and/or file argumnents "
		       "(seperated by spaces) ie; ./rot2 3 string file.txt\n");
		exit(EXIT_FAILURE);	
	}

	// Initial rotation
	int rotby = 13;
	FILE *fp;
			
	for (int i = 1; i < argc; i++)
	{
		// For loop index
		int length = strlen(argv[i]);
		// Try to open file argument
		fp = fopen(argv[i], "r");
		
		// If file could be opened then pass
		// file to processFile function	
		if (fp)
		{
			processFile(fp);
			// Finish processing by closing file
			fclose(fp);
		}
		// Check if first char is digit
		// If so set new rotation
		else if (isdigit(argv[i][0]))
		{
			rotby = atoi(argv[i]); 
		}
		// Cipher each char of the argument string
		// print results to stdout
		else if (!isdigit(argv[i][0]))
		{	
			for (int j = 0; j < length; j++)
			{
				printf("%d  %c  %c\n", j, argv[i][j], rotate(rotby, argv[i][j]));
			}
		}
		printf("\n");
	}		
	exit(EXIT_SUCCESS);
}

Hi Chris,

I know you mentioned the linux subsystem on windows 10, but I thought I would post how to
set it up for those who want to use a windows machine but the unix command line.

Personally I find it quite useful to quickly type "bash" in the start menu then its up and
running, but for reference it does have its limitations (for instance can't access all
hardware, including USB's), so I do also have Ubuntu set up in virtualbox for the full unix
os. And of course ssh to the macs at uni.

Hope this is useful for someone. You will need admin rights and a decent internet
connection. You will also need relatively recent windows updates (anniversary update) and
64-bit architecture.

1. Open the settings app -> update and security -> developer mode. 
2. Search for "turn windows features on or off" or open it in control panel (programs and
features). Find "Windows Subsystem for Linux (beta)" and check it. Reboot if prompted.
3. Type "bash" into start menu or find it in the apps list. Follow the prompts, create root
(sudo) password.
4. Optional - install vim. Go to the exe file at
https://ftp.nluug.nl/pub/vim/pc/gvim80-586.exe and install. Open bash then type vim to run.

Your home folder (~) is at C:\Users\User\AppData\Local\lxss\home\user and you won't see
\lxss when browsing in file explorer, you'll need to type it in the address bar.

Hope this was useful for someone!

Previously, <poster101@student.uwa.edu.au> wrote:

> However it always prints a question mark ("?") at the end of a file (this doesn't happen 
> when I input text in the CLI).
> 
> I'd appreciate it if someone could take a look at my code:
>

>     while (ch != EOF) {
>       // read character in ch, returns an int
>       ch = fgetc(file_read);
>       printf("%c", rotate(ch, ROT));
>     }


Hi Tommaso,

The problem is caused by the bit of your code I've left, above.

*IF* there was a special character at the end of each file, represented by the constant -1 and 
referred to by EOF, then you'd still be printing out that character, once, before leaving the 
while loop.  It's probably not printing an actual question mark character, but using the 
question mark to indicate that it's outputting a character that it can't display.

Another way to write it could be:

      while(true) {
        // read character in ch, returns an int
        ch = fgetc(file_read);
        if(cc == EOF) {
          break;
        }
        printf("%c", rotate(ch, ROT));
      }

HOWEVER, there is no such special character at the end of the file, so a better way to do this 
is to test for the *condition* of being at the end-of-file using a standard predicate declared 
in the <stdio.h> header file:

      while( ! feof(file_read) ) {
        ......
      }

Hope this helps,

Subject: How I did 9 on Lab Sheet 2 (Roman Numerals)
From: poster096@student.uwa.edu.au

Here's my code for question 9. Feedback would be much 
appreciated. :)

    #include<stdio.h>
    #include<stdlib.h>
    #include<stdbool.h>
    #include<math.h>
    #include<string.h>
    
    char one;
    char five;
    char ten;
    
    int numDigits(int k);
    
    int nthDigit(int n, int k);
    
    void asRoman(int digit, int column);
    
    int main(int argc, char *argv[])
    {
      if(argc != 2)
      {
        printf("Expected one argument, got %i", argc - 1);
        exit(EXIT_FAILURE);
      }
      int num = atoi(argv[1]);
      for(int i = numDigits(num); i > 0; i--)   //for each digit we find the roman numeral 
representation
      {
        int digit = nthDigit(i, num);           //isolate the ith digit
        asRoman(digit, i - 1);                  //we will later use digit * 10^(i-1)
      }
      printf("\n");
      exit(EXIT_SUCCESS);
    	return 0;
    }
    
    int numDigits(int k)
    {
      int count = 1;
      while(k /= 10)
      {
        count++;
      }
      return count;
    }
    
    int nthDigit(int n, int k)       //the first digit of 123 is 3, second is 2, etc
    {
      while(n > 1)
      {
        k /= 10;
        n--;
      }
      return (k % 10);
    }
    
    void asRoman(int digit, int col)   //1 if the digit comes from the 1s column, 2 for the 
10s column, etc
    {
      int k = (int) (digit*pow(10, col));
      if(k < 10)
      {
        one = 'I';
        five = 'V';
        ten = 'X';
      }
      else if(k < 100)
      {
        one = 'X';
        five = 'L';
        ten = 'C';
      }
      else if(k < 1000)
      {
        one = 'C';
        five = 'D';
        ten = 'M';
      }
      else   //we can only express numbers like 3000, 4000, 5000 with Ms
      {
        for(int i = 0; i < digit; i++)
        {
          printf("M");
        }
        return;
      }
      switch(digit)   
      {
        case 1:
        {
          printf("%c", one);
          break;
        }
        case 2:
        {
          printf("%c%c", one, one);
          break;
        }
        case 3:
        {
          printf("%c%c%c", one, one, one);
          break;
        }
        case 4:
        {
          printf("%c%c", one, five);
          break;
        }
        case 5:
        {
          printf("%c", five);
          break;
        }
        case 6:
        {
          printf("%c%c", five, one);
          break;
        }
        case 7:
        {
          printf("%c%c%c", five, one, one);
          break;
        }
        case 8:
        {
          printf("%c%c%c%c", five, one, one, one);
          break;
        }
        case 9:
        {
          printf("%c%c", one, ten);
          break;
        }
        case 10:
        {
          printf("%c", ten);
          break;
        }
      }
    }

The program makes sure that it doesn't discriminate between upper and lowercase
letters. It also disregards any non-letters.

#include <stdio.h>
#include <stdlib.h>
#include <string.h>
#include <ctype.h>

int main(int argc, char *argv[])
{
        printf("There are: \n");
        for(char c = 'A'; c <= 'Z'; ++c)
        {
                int count = 0;
                for(int i = 1; i < argc; ++i)
                {
                        for(int j = 0; j < strlen(argv[i]); ++j)
                        {
                                if (putchar(toupper(argv[i][j]) == c))
                                {
                                        ++count;
                                }
                        }
                }
                if (count == 0) {}

                else
                {
                        printf("%i %c's  ", count, c);
                }
        }
                printf("\nin this sentence. \n");


}

Heres my attempt at q10 of lab 2. It works for the test cases i gave it but i feel 
its super inefficient and could be coded better. Any suggestions how it could be 
coded better or if its broken or something? 
Thank you! :D  

#include <stdio.h>
#include <stdlib.h>
#include <string.h>
#include <ctype.h>
#include <math.h>


char alpha[26];
int count[26];

void makebet(){
    int i=0;
    for(char j='a';j<='z';j++){
    alpha[i]=j;
    i++;
    }
}

void add(char c){
    for(int i=0;i<26;i++){
        if(isalpha(c) && (alpha[i]==c||(isupper(c) && alpha[i]==c+32))){
        count[i] ++;
        }
    }
}

char* combine(char* s1,char* s2){
    int l1= strlen(s1);
    int l2=strlen(s2);
    char a[l1+l2+2];
    for(int i=0;i<l1+l2+1;i++){
        if(i<l1){
            a[i]=s1[i];
        }else if(i==l1){ a[i]=' ';
        }else{
             a[i]=s2[i-l1-1];
        }
    }
    a[l1+l2 +1]='\0';
    char* b=a;
    return b;
}

int findlast(){
    for(int i=25;i>-1;i--)if(count[i]!=0)return i;
    return -1;
}

int findSecLast(){
    int end=findlast();
     for(int i=end-1;i>-1;i--)if(count[i]!=0)return i;
    return -1;
}
//took the lazy assumption that there will be at most 99 of a certain char. 
char* numtostr(int d){
    char* base[19]={ "one", "two", "three", "four", "five", "six",
            "seven", "eight", "nine", "ten", "eleven", "twelve",
            "thirteen", "fourteen", "fifteen", "sixteen", "seventeen",
            "eighteen", "nineteen" };
    char* carry[8]={"twenty", "thirty", "forty", "fifty",
            "sixty", "seventy", "eighty", "ninety" };
    if(d<20){
    return base[d-1];
    }else{
        int bias=d/10; 
        int follow= d%10;
        return combine(carry[bias-2],base[follow-1]);
    }
}

int main(int argc,char* argv[]){
    makebet();
    if(argc<2){
        fprintf(stderr,"NO Input BRUHHHH\n");
        exit(EXIT_FAILURE);
    }
    for (int i=1;i<argc;i++){
        int len= strlen(argv[i]);
        for(int j=0;j<len;j++) add(argv[i][j]);
    }
    int end=findlast();
    int nekminit=findSecLast();
    printf("This Sentence has ");
    //probs could be written better
    for(int i=0;i<26;i++){
        if(count[i]!=0 && !(i==end || i==nekminit)){
           printf("%s %c's, ",numtostr(count[i]),alpha[i]);
        }else if(i==nekminit){
            printf("%s %c's ",numtostr(count[i]),alpha[i]);
        }else if(i==end){
            printf("and %s %c's  ",numtostr(count[i]),alpha[i]);
        }
    }
    printf("\n");
}

//end

  {

Input - Print Credit Card: 1234567890987654

         Sum = 52

         Sum = 320
         Sum = 372
         Sum = 422

Date: Thu Aug 24 11:45:23 2017
To: help2002@csse.uwa.edu.au
Received: from 130.95.1.70
Subject: Lab 3 Question 6
From: chris.mcdonald@uwa.edu.au
X-smilie: none
X-img: none

ANONYMOUS wrote:

> Can someone please explain how i am getting the output? What i was trying to do is 
> to go through the array backwards, starting from the last int in the array and 
> take that number and add it to sum and so on. I'm not sure why i get the first 
> output as 52.


The first value you get is 52 because you're adding together the internal, ASCII, representations of 
each character, and not the digits that you hope you're seeing.
The internal representation of the character '4' is 52  (run  man ascii,  see the decimal set)

If you wish the value 52 to be more useful to you, you need to subtract from it the internal 
representation of the digit '0':   (creditCard[i] - '0')
which will work without us having to know the actual internal values of each character - just 
assuming that '4' is a distance of four from '0', as we'd hope.

Also, the value  strlen(creditCard)
never changes throughout your program, so don't calculate it 3 times :-)

#include<stdio.h>
#include<stdlib.h>
#include<string.h>

int creditCard(char creditCard[])
{
    int sumO = 0, sumE = 0,sum;
    int length = strlen(creditCard);
    if(length>= 13 && length <= 16)
    {
      for(int i = length - 1; i > 0; i = i - 2)
      //for(int i = 0; i < strlen(creditCard); i+=2)
      {
        sumO = sumO + creditCard[i] - '0'; 
        //sumO = sumO + creditCard[strlen(creditCard) - 1 - i] - '0';
      }

      for(int i = length - 2; i > 0; i = i - 2)
      {
        int prod = (creditCard[i] - '0')*2;

        if(prod > 9)
        {
          int add = prod % 10 + prod /10;
          sumE = sumE + add;
        }
        else
        {
          sumE = sumE + prod;
        }
        sum = sumE + sumO;
      }

      if(sum % 10 == 0)
      {
        printf("Valid Credit Card\n");
      }
      else
      {
        printf("Credit Card Not Valid\n");
      }
    }
    else
    {
      printf("not valid\n");
    }
    return 0;
}

int main(int argv, char *argc[])
{
  if(argv != 2)
  {
    printf("Error Message");
    exit(EXIT_FAILURE);
  }
  else
  {
    int results = creditCard(argc[1]);
    printf("Results: %d", results);
    exit(EXIT_SUCCESS);
  }
  return 0;
}

ANONYMOUS wrote:
>    while( s[i] != '\0' && p[i] != '\0')

This is the line in which the error is really occurring (Apart from the non-
initialization of the variable i pointed out by Chris). What you are doing is 
traversing both the p and s char arrays until a null byte is reached. Given your s 
array is equal to "s", the while loop exits right after the completion of the 1st 
iteration. You would like to traverse only the p array copying the contents across to 
the s array.

functions in main instead of writing all the code there?

statements to output what was requested. Thanks.

Date: Tue Aug 29 20:27:07 2017
To: help2002@csse.uwa.edu.au
Received: from 106.68.14.237
Subject: "Performing minimum amount of work in main function"
From: poster037@student.uwa.edu.au
X-smilie: none
X-img: none

I interpret it to mean something along the lines of minimizing the amount of code logic 
contained within the main function. In other words, calling other user defined functions 
from the main function is a more effective approach and introduces cleaner code, 
maintainability, and re-usability.

For example:

void functionA();
void functionB();
void printString(char* str);

int main(int argc, char** argv)
{
    functionA();
    functionB();
    printString(*argv);
    printString(*(argv+1));
    etc.

    return EXIT_SUCCESS;
}

void functionA()
{
    //5 lines of code here.
}

void functionB()
{
    //10 lines of code here.
}

void printString(char* str)
{
    //3 lines of code here.
}

This is more readable and far easier to maintain than this:

int main(int argc, char** argv)
{
    Code for functionA (5 lines)
    Code for functionB (10 lines)
    Code for printing 1st element of argv (3 lines)
    Code for printing 2nd element of argv (3 lines)
    etc.

    return EXIT_SUCCESS;
}

As you can see, the main function is now cluttered with many different functions serving 
different purposes as well as containing the logic for each of those different functions. 
It becomes a lot harder to modify the code and to ensure that the program doesn't break 
from modifications. In the scope of these labs, this isn't such an issue but when you 
start to develop larger programs, the format of the first example is far superior and 
will save you from many potential issues that can arise.

Here's my code for this question. Ignore the swap/insert functions. Main isn't really 
important either. I'm getting a bus error in the fill_array function whenever I try to 
use atoi(). Does anyone know why this is happening? Chris?




#include<stdio.h>
#include<stdlib.h>
#include<stdbool.h>
#include<math.h>
#include<string.h>
#include<ctype.h>

int number_array[10000];

void fill_array(int numbers[], char string[]);

void next_number(char string[], int offset, char* dest);

void insert(int num_array[], int number);

void swap(int num_array[], int pos1, int pos2);


int main(int argc, char *argv[])
{
	if(argc < 2)
	{
		printf("Expected 1 argument, got 0");
		exit(EXIT_FAILURE);
	}
	else
	{
		fill_array(number_array, argv[1]);
		int i = 0;
		while(number_array[i] != 0)
		{
			printf("number is %i\n", number_array[i]);
		}
		exit(EXIT_SUCCESS);
	}
	return 0;
}

void fill_array(int numbers[], char string[])
{
	int pos = 0;
	while(string[pos] != '\0')
	{
		char digits[10];
		next_number(string, pos, digits); 
		printf("Number was read as %s\n", digits);
		pos += strlen(digits);
		printf("length of number is %lu\n", strlen(digits));                 
		int num = atoi(digits);  //BUS ERROR IS OCCURRING ON THIS LINE
		printf("num is %i", num);                  
		if(string[pos] == '\0')
		{
			insert(numbers, num);
			break;                                
		}
		else if(string[pos] == ',')
		{
			insert(numbers, num);
		}
		else   //its a dash
		{
			pos++;
			char next_digits[10];
			next_number(string, pos, next_digits);
			pos += strlen(next_digits);
			int next_num = atoi(next_digits);
			for(int i = num; i <= next_num; i++)
			{
				insert(numbers, i);
			}
		}
		pos++;
	}
}

void next_number(char string[], int offset, char* dest) 
{
	int i = offset;
	while(isdigit(string[i]) && string[i] != '\0')
	{
		dest[i] = string[i];
		printf("Number written was %c\n", dest[i]);
		i++;
	}
	dest[i+1] = '\0';
}

void insert(int num_array[], int number)
{
	int i = 0;
	while(num_array[i] < number)
	{
		i++;
	}
	if(number == num_array[i])
	{
		//do nothing, we don't want duplicate numbers
	}
	else
	{
		int x = i;
		{
			while(num_array[x] != 0)
			{
				swap(num_array, x, x + 1);
				x++;
			}
		}
		number_array[i] = number;
	}
}

void swap(int num_array[], int pos1, int pos2)
{
	int tmp = num_array[pos1];
	num_array[pos1] = num_array[pos2];
	num_array[pos2] = tmp;
}

The first error message tells you where the error is located and occurs when you try 
to pass the temp variable into the strcpy function (Argument 1 as it states).

The rest of the error messages tell you what is actually causing the error and where 
other instances of the error(s) are located.

It states that you are passing an incompatible variable type to the function. The 
function is looking for a pointer that is of type char (You will learn more about this 
in week 8 looking at the timetable), and you are providing it a 5 element array 
pointer of type char. If you change the input argument to temp[0] (or using pointers, 
*temp), it should fix the problem with the incompatible input types and also ensures 
that you are pointing to the correct memory location for the data you are modifying.

To: help2002@csse.uwa.edu.au
Received: from 130.95.1.70
Subject: Lab-3:  Why subtract the value '0' ?
From: chris.mcdonald@uwa.edu.au
X-smilie: none
X-img: none

Someone emailed me this question:

I am running through the solutions for Lab sheet 3 and was wondering in the below code, 
what the action of the  ( - ‘0’ ) has on digit

Thanks!

  for(int i = strlen(creditcard)-1 ; i >= 0  ; i = i-1) {
        char digit = creditcard[i] - '0';

        if(odd) {
            s1 += digit;
        ....
   }

______
 From each character in the character array creditcard, we subtract the value of the 
character zero, written as '0', to determine the integer represented by each character.

For example,  if an array element was the character '4', then the expression
('4' - '0') has the integer value of 4.
Thus we're "converting" from the single character to the integer it's representing.

We've done something similar when manipulating lowercase characters:  (ch - 'a') way back 
in Labsheet-1.

You'll again need this same code in Q1 of Labsheet-4.

Date: Sun Sep  3 00:48:03 2017
Edit: Sun Sep  3 00:52:20 2017
To: help2002@csse.uwa.edu.au
Received: from 106.69.133.128
Subject: mid-test 2016 Q8 void question
From: poster037@student.uwa.edu.au
X-smilie: none
X-img: none

A function of type void is just a function that does not return a value. This does 
not mean that it can not have return statements.

A simple example:

void function(int number)
{
    if (number < 0)
    {
        return;
    }
    if (number > 10)
    {
        return;
    }
    etc.
    
    printf("Number is %d \n", number);
}

For an example this code is fine, but it is best to avoid splitting similar 
conditionals and combining them into a single if statement:

    if (number < 0 || number > 10)

The return statements basically act like an exit from the function similar in 
functionality to that of a break statement in a loop. If the input number is less 
than 0 or more than 10, the function exits early without continuing on to print the 
number. In other words, once a function sees a return statement, it has found its 
solution or requirement and just stops itself from executing any code beyond that 
return statement.

You can have as many return statements as you like.

Subject: My solution to the cards question from lab 4
From: poster096@student.uwa.edu.au
X-smilie: none
X-img: none
X-anonymous: yes

Represents a deck of cards as the numbers 1 - 52. 1 - 13 are the hearts, 14 - 26 the 
diamonds, etc. Also, the order of values in my code is KA23456...

void shuffle_deck(int deck[])
{
	for(int pos = 0; pos < 52; pos++)
	{
		int random = rand() % (52-pos) + pos;
		int tmp = deck[pos];
		deck[pos] = deck[random];
		deck[random] = tmp;
	}
}


void fill_deck(int deck[])
{
	for(int i = 0; i < 52; i++)
	{	
		deck[i] = i + 1;
	}
}


void printcard(int number)
{
	int suit = (number - 1) / 13;
	int value = number % 13;
	printf("%c%c\n", getvalue(value), getsuit(suit));
}

char getsuit(int suit)
{
	return "HDCS"[suit];
}

char getvalue(int value)
{
	return "KA23456789TJQ"[value];
}

int main(int argc, char *argv[])
{
	srand(getpid());
	int deck[52];
	fill_deck(deck);
	shuffle_deck(deck);
	for(int i = 0; i < 52; i++)
	{
		printcard(deck[i]);
	}
        return 0;
}

Subject: I'm sick of typing so much of the same stuff
From: poster083@student.uwa.edu.au
X-smilie: none
X-img: none

Thought i'd share this seeing as it's helped me get through the labs a bit quicker 
and easier.

To streamline the task solving process I codemonkey'd a program that can generate a 
typical C file we use when writing solutions. Just write a file titled 'generate.c' 
or similar that looks something like this:


   #!/bin/bash
   #run the program with bash

   cat << EOF

   //code that you want in your typical file, e.g:

   #include <stdio.h>
   #include <stdlib.h>

   int main(int argc, char *argv[]) {

       return 0;
   }
   EOF


 From there, you can create an alias of the directory of that file to something easy 
to type. Mine was located in ~/Documents/CITS2002, so I wrote:
alias gen="~/Documents/CITS2002/generate.c >";

the > funnels the output of the generate program into a filename that you type 
after it

To make sure that alias (and any others you want) stay permanent, modify your 
~/.profile file with the alias command. (I think this was in the lectures/labs?)

Please let me know if any of this is wrong/not up to standard

From: chris.mcdonald@uwa.edu.au
X-smilie: none
X-img: none

Some questions emailed directly to me - answers for all!
________

Q: Do 3 byte MAC addresses become short forms for a list of 6 byte MAC addresses?

No; the 3-byte OUI prefix simply indicates with vendor made the WiFi card used in the 
transmission.  Vendor pay a licence/royalty fee to the IEEE to adopt their WiFi 
standard(s), and a vendor may license ('own') several different OUIs;  e.g. Samsung owns 
493 OUIs in the large OUI file.

 
Q: Is this a standard for companies to say that all their computers MAC addresses contain 
the first 3 byte MAC addresses?

Yes, pay your money and you, too, can have your own OUIs:

  https://standards.ieee.org/develop/regauth/oui/index.html


Q: Is there an include statement for sort?

No, include files provide prototypes for functions - our project is not using a C99 
function to sort its output, but using an external program.  Suggest that you listen to 
today's workshop recording.

(P.S. there is a standard C99 function named qsort(), but we're not using it)

From: poster066@student.uwa.edu.au

Have just been trying to parse my data into 3 arrays for sorting. But have been 
running into problems with splitting the strings and putting them into arrays. I 
keep getting segmentation fault errors. Any ideas?

void tPacketSum(char filename[]){
    FILE *data;
    char line[LINESIZE];

    data = fopen(filename, "r");
    if(data == NULL){
        printf("error in accessing file specified \n");
        exit(EXIT_FAILURE);
    }
    printf("access success\n");

    int i = 0;

    while(fgets(line, sizeof line, data) != NULL){
        i++;
    }
    printf("Number of entries: %i  \n", i);

    fclose(data);
    printf("1\n");
    data = fopen(filename, "r\n");
    printf("1\n");

    char* transMAC[i];
    char* recMAC[i];
    int bytes[i];
    int j = 0;
    while(fgets(line, sizeof line, data) != NULL){
        strtok(line, "\t");
        strcpy(transMAC[j],strtok(NULL, "\t"));
        strcpy(recMAC[j],strtok(NULL, "\t"));
        bytes[j] = atoi(strtok(NULL, "\t"));
        j++;
    }

    for(int k = 0; k < i; k++){
        printf("%s   ", transMAC[k]);
        printf("%s   ", recMAC[k]);
        printf("%i   \n", bytes[k]);
    }


}

Date: Sun Sep 10 11:23:07 2017
To: help2002@csse.uwa.edu.au
Received: from 106.69.139.110
Subject: Project 1: MAC address formats
From: poster020@student.uwa.edu.au
X-smilie: none
X-img: none

Can't answer the question, but just quick correction:
The OUIs are not space separated, they are dash separated.

On another note, you should probably never assume that users are going to be perfectly
compliant when you write software, or that data will never deviate from one format. It
would be better (and honestly not much extra work, and no extra complexity) to write
your program such that it deals correctly with any valid format (i.e.
lowercase/uppercase and dash-separated/colon-separated) for both packets and OUIs. It's
really just a few extra checks.

You can attain full marks for the project from the concepts presented to the end of Week-6.

documentation to use the introductory concepts correctly.

 
> For example, you have not covered pointers and addresses yet but my solution uses them.

> solution?

The sample solution is 360 lines long, including 65 blank lines and 35 comment-only lines.
It uses no pointers, other that the parameter in a single call to wait().
It's deliberately written in a way that I believe all students should be able to write.

The project is not seeking any degree of efficiency in terms of speed or size, but should not be 
wasteful in its coding or excessively large data structures.

Date: Mon Sep 11 13:10:28 2017
To: help2002@csse.uwa.edu.au
Received: from 10.20.153.217
Subject: Ignore ALL lines with broadcast address
From: poster029@student.uwa.edu.au
X-smilie: none
X-img: none

Hi all,

When I went to test my implementation of wifistats I had to do a lot of trial and error to 
figure out what was going on, and I eventually realised the problem came down to how I was 
treating lines with broadcast MAC addresses.

When I read the spec, I assumed that you only had to ignore a line if the MAC address we 
were listing by was a broadcast address (i.e. the 2nd column if 't' was set in the command 
line args, the 3rd if it was 'r').

In actuality, you have to ignore a line if either of the MAC addresses are broadcast, 
regardless of what the program is listing by.

Just thought I'd put this clarification out there in case anyone else is scratching their 
heads over why their program is giving different results to the reference implementation.

Date: Mon Sep 11 13:24:44 2017
To: help2002@csse.uwa.edu.au
Received: from 10.20.192.83
Subject: Ignore ALL lines with broadcast address
From: poster020@student.uwa.edu.au
X-smilie: none
X-img: none

Hi Alexander,

It doesn't make sense to me for the source address to be a broadcast address. I think if a line
has a broadcast address in it, it's going to be the recipient address. If I'm sending a packet,
it makes sense for me to say I want to send it to everyone (so I'm the transmitter, and the
recipient is the broadcast address). I can't see how it would make sense to say I've received a
packet from everyone (i.e. the transmitter address being a broadcast).

So I think, in reality, you are supposed to ignore packets sent to the broadcast address, which
you weren't doing originally in the case of 't' being set in your command line arguments. Hence
why your output was incorrect.

Date: Mon Sep 11 13:26:01 2017
To: help2002@csse.uwa.edu.au

Subject: Ignore ALL lines with broadcast address
From: chris.mcdonald@uwa.edu.au
X-smilie: none
X-img: none

Hi Alex,

Just to clarify something - the MAC addresses we're seeing in our project are of two types: either 
unique MAC addresses belonging to a single device, or broadcast addresses (ff:ff:....) which 
collectively describe all addresses.  While it makes sense for the broadcast address to be listed as 
the destination/receiver of WiFi traffic,  you'll never see it as a transmitter's address.

Date: Wed Sep 13 04:32:51 2017
To: help2002@csse.uwa.edu.au
Received: from 144.136.135.176
Subject: Assignment 1: Intended method of passing data to unix sort (and necessity of forking?)
From: poster063@student.uwa.edu.au
X-smilie: none
X-img: none

> Also, I'm a little bit confused about the necessecity of forking our process before 
> exec'ing sort. It seems like all the formatting and filtering should be done before 
> sort is needed, and sort doesn't need to return anything, so is there a specific 
> reason for the fork? Without anything more to do I would think that after the fork 
> the parent would simply terminate.

The reason why we are forking our process is because we still have operations to do after
invoking /usr/bin/sort (namely printing our newly sorted data to stdout). The execve()
family of commands do not necessarily return on success, instead the process that calls
execve() has its own process terminated (see https://linux.die.net/man/2/execve and note
that the text, data, bss and stack of the parent are overwritten). The required command to
be used is execv(), which is a front-end for execve() (which means that it just
reinterprets its input parameters to pass to execve()) also does not return on success,
and thus should be forked.

Of course, not all execv() and execve() implementations terminate the current process (I
know on my computer it doesn't), so you might want to call exit() after execv().

With regards to passing data to /usr/bin/sort, I'm currently using a temporary file,
however the semantics are very similar with pipes (see
http://tldp.org/LDP/lpg/node11.html). As I only have easy access to a linux machine at
home, I'm not sure whether the method would be identical on a mac (though I suspect that
it would be as they are both Unix based and POSIX compliant).

 - Tom

Previously, <poster049@student.uwa.edu.au> wrote:

> Hi all,
> 
> I was wondering if anyone can help, I am trying to pass the following arguments to sort 
> using execv() 
> 
> "-t" "$'\t'" "-rnk2" "stats.txt"
> 
> These work directly from the terminal to sort the text file, however when trying to pass 
> them through my function the $'\t' doesn't go through correctly, I assume this is because I 
> am trying to pass the separator \t.
> 
> Anyone got any tips how to navigate this problem?

You are right - C will take the '\t' character in a string and parse that as a tab, regardless of 
how many quotation marks you put around it. 

You can escape slashes using an extra backslash; e.g. '\\t' should print '\t'.

Date: Sat Sep 16 04:39:28 2017
To: help2002@csse.uwa.edu.au
Received: from 106.68.243.246
Subject: Assignment 1: Intended method of passing data to unix sort (and necessity of forking?)
From: poster003@student.uwa.edu.au
X-smilie: none
X-img: none

Previously, <poster072@student.uwa.edu.au> wrote:

> Yeah Jesse, I was having a think and if you're piping some input to sort then maybe the parent needs to stay open to 
> pipe the data through.

I've been doing a lot of reading and testing, and I think for the purposes of this assignment we *technically* wouldn't 
need to fork to get the output we're looking for, but it seems like it's generally considered bad practice to exec without 
forking. Keeping the parent around allows us to monitor the child exec'd process to ensure it completed successfully, and 
perhaps to clean up or retry if it fails.

As far as anonymous pipes go, I believe they stay open until they're no longer linked to a file descriptor, but they can 
only hold so much in their buffer (something like 16-128KiB depending on how the system is configured?) before they block. 
As long as you're not shifting much data, even without forking you could probably do some nasty stuff like:

int fd[2];
pipe(fd);
fprintf(fd[0], "format", ...); // writing to the input of the pipe
dup2(fd[1], 0); // duping the output of the pipe to stdin
execv();

I haven't been able to test this myself yet, but I have to imagine that FDs remain when a process is exec'd. If they 
weren't, I'm pretty sure you'd never be able to pipe to stdin (e.g. using | in bash) without root permissions?

Date: Sat Sep 16 04:57:29 2017
To: help2002@csse.uwa.edu.au
Received: from 106.68.243.246
Subject: calling /usr/bin/sort using exec()
From: poster003@student.uwa.edu.au
X-smilie: none
X-img: none

ANONYMOUS wrote:

> Previously, <poster080@student.uwa.edu.au> wrote:
> 
> > just adding to that, use man execve for further clarification.
> 
> Thanks for clarifying. So i have read the manual, and from what i understand, the sort must 
> read from a text file to sort it? So does it mean we have to put the addresses and the 
> bytes that has been summed up together (not yet sorted), into a single text file and then 
> use sort to do its thing? 
> 
> Cheers

For this purposes of this project I think we want to use "execv()", execve() is the raw system 
call, and there are a few different C library wrappers for it.

The differences between all the different wrappers are mostly just the way they handle arguments. 
Make sure you read the manual page for execv ("man execv" at the terminal), because it's 
different from the manual page for execve (This confused me for a long time).

There's also a man page for sort which describes how it works (it's actually surprisingly 
powerful and can sort lines of text by collums etc). If you're unsure how you can try mocking up 
a text file and then running sort on it directly to figure out what arguments you need to then 
feed into execv() to get it to play nice.

Date: Sun Sep 17 00:06:28 2017

Subject: sort() unusual results
From: poster039@student.uwa.edu.au
X-smilie: none
X-img: none

Fixed. For anyone who ran into the same issue execv should take your arguments as execv(args[0], 
args) rather than execv("usr/bin/sort", args)

Date: Sun Sep 17 10:32:26 2017
To: help2002@csse.uwa.edu.au
Received: from 101.186.137.29
Subject: More sort help
From: poster026@student.uwa.edu.au
X-smilie: none
X-img: none
X-anonymous: yes

Try adding the -d flag (--dictionary-order) for the second field. 

I believe the sort field, when specified by a delimiter (or by default), will include all
characters including punctuation, whitespace etc. So by including the -d flag it should sort
only on alphanumeric characters.

Alternatively you could try adding the -i flag (--ignore-nonprinting) which will ignore
whitespace... but I think this may be overridden if -d is present.

Don't know if this will help but worth a shot!

Date: Sun Sep 17 21:35:10 2017
To: help2002@csse.uwa.edu.au
Received: from 58.6.73.25
Subject: sort() unusual results
From: poster003@student.uwa.edu.au
X-smilie: none
X-img: none

ANONYMOUS wrote:

> Previously, <poster039@student.uwa.edu.au> wrote:
> 
> > Fixed. For anyone who ran into the same issue execv should take your arguments as execv(args[0], 
> > args) rather than execv("usr/bin/sort", args)
> 
> so the args[] = {"/usr/bin/sort",(option...),(filename),NULL}?
> I also struggle with this one..

That's basically it, but execv() expects a pointer-to-a-char-array (char *args[]), not a char-array 
(char args[]).

It helps to keep in mind that when you use an array as an argument it decays to a pointer to the first 
element. So an argument of type (char args[]) will decay to type (char *args) and type (char *args[]) 
will decay to type (char **args).

Date: Sun Sep 17 21:42:33 2017

Yep, we should be able to use any library as long as it compiles on the iMacs in the lab. 
So basically anything in the C99 standard library should be fine. If we couldn't import 
<string.h> this assignment would be a nightmare haha.

You can basically assume any of the C99 headers here are avaliable: 
https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/C_standard_library

But, you'll also probably need to use <unistd.h> which is where all the POSIX syscalls 
are.

Date: Tue Sep 19 09:06:02 2017
To: help2002@csse.uwa.edu.au
Received: from 10.20.237.9
Subject: maximum number of distinct MAC addresses - 500. 
From: poster014@student.uwa.edu.au
X-smilie: none
X-img: none

> 
> by the way I do not under stand why We have to check this.
> even if there are just ten distinctive MAC address the number of
> row in the input data can be over million given time.

This is to help you with choosing the size of what data type you are choosing to store 
the distinct MAC addresses. For example, if you are storing each distinct mac address as 
a string in an array, you can assume the size of that array will only be 500 distinct 
elements long.

Date: Tue Sep 19 18:57:14 2017
To: help2002@csse.uwa.edu.au

From: chris.mcdonald@uwa.edu.au

X-img: none

ANONYMOUS wrote:

> In my code I have a 2D array of characters:
> 
> my_array[MAX_MACS][17];
> 
> Is this declaration statement supposed to initialise everything in it to the null byte?
> I made this assumption in my code and it led to disastrous results. 
> 
> Ryan you were helping me after the lab this afternoon. Just letting you and everyone else 
> know I solved the problem using 
> 
> my_array[MAX_MACS][17] = {{0}};

Global variables, declared outside of all functions, are initiated with zeroes.
In (nearly) all other cases, variables are not automatically initialised.

Subject: Is order important in "r" or "t"?
From: poster013@student.uwa.edu.au
X-smilie: none
X-img: none

A good rule of thumb is that the sample solution output is the desired output, order 
and all.
However, a closer examination of the project brief may help to clarify things:
"The goal of this project is to write a C99 program, named wifistats, that reports 
the number of bytes either transmitted or received by devices using WiFi. The 
program may be requested to generate reports sorted by either the device MACs or by 
the vendors' names, from the largest transmitter (most bytes transmitted) to the 
smallest."

This implicates that, after sorting by the total bytes transmitted/received, one 
should then sort by MAC address or Vendor name (Depending on the mode of operation).

Date: Wed Sep 20 13:45:10 2017
To: help2002@csse.uwa.edu.au
Received: from 10.20.237.9
Subject: IMPORTANT: Reading in the filename
From: poster014@student.uwa.edu.au
X-smilie: none
X-img: none

Hi all, 

Something that has confused a lot of people in the project is the way you are 
expected to read in a file. 

fopen does NOT require a file to have '.txt' appended at the end of it. The reason 
why the example instructions give:

    $./wifistats r sample-packets

And not

    $./wifitstats r sample-packets.txt

Is simply because the file has been saved onto the demonstration machine as 
"sample-packets". Not "sample-packets.txt". 

NOTE: If you are concatenating ".txt" every time you read in a file in your code, 
this will cause errors when we test it, as you will be adding a '.txt' to the 
filename when that filename might already have '.txt', and thus it won't exist. 

If you pass a file name to the program that does not exist on the computer, it will 
not work. Because it shouldn't work. If you have the file saved as "sample-
packets.txt", passing in "sample-packets" as an argument won't work if you don't 
also have that file on our computer - this is expected. 

Hypothetically, we should be able to give you a sample file, with MAC addresses in 
it, called "frodo.bagins", and it should still work  (provided the file 
"frodo.bagins" exists in the directory the code is running). 

Please let me know if you have any questions on this matter. 

Cheers, 

Ryan [Lab guy]

Date: Thu Sep 21 23:52:09 2017
To: help2002@csse.uwa.edu.au
Received: from 180.149.251.122
Subject: How slow is too slow.
From: poster014@student.uwa.edu.au
X-smilie: none
X-img: none

ANONYMOUS wrote:

> Indeed. I've reduced it to about 30 seconds though. 
> It's the processing of the packet line which is doing it. Though I'm sure I'm not doing anything 
> wasteful. I've tried both sscanf and strtok and they give take about the same time. Perhaps it's 
> writing to globals rather than parameters that's doing it? Like maybe globals don't get loaded onto 
> the CPU registrar or something. I don't know. Will we get marked down for taking too long?

30 seconds does seem *long-ish*, but it is difficult to tell exactly why without seeing it in run.

I would try running this on the macOS machines to see if there is any difference in performance - 
especially given we shall be compiling and marking the code on those machines. 

I doubt it is globals affecting your performance in this context; it is most likely looping or 
conditionals that will result in longer run-times for an assignment like this. 

Provided the run time of the code is not in the order of minutes - i.e. if it's spitting out the right 
answer, but only after 15 minutes of computing, there is definitely something wrong - I would suggest that 
there would be no marks lost in the "automated testing" scenario, provided the output is correct. However, 
when we look through the code for readability and good programming practise, we might find design flaws 
that are contributing to the run time, and mark accordingly. 

I would suggest for your situation, checking it on a Macintosh ASAP is the best idea, and not to stress if 
it is still taking 30-ish seconds. :) 

Cheers, 

Ryan [Lab guy]

    Arrays that are excessively large.
    Null bytes on the end of character arrays (Make sure they're there).

Also, when tracking down Abort Traps or Segmentation Faults, fprintf to stderr is much more reliable.

Previously, <poster098@student.uwa.edu.au> wrote:

> Well the alternative approach that my partner and I have come up with involves the 
> use of global variables (which is often considered bad practice, unless absolutely 
> necessary). While it is clear that repetitive code can cause problems when 
> debugging/modifying code, our alternative approach also has its issues. Having said 
> that, it seems that removing duplicate code may be the better option.

Welp, maybe this is a bit late, but as David said, have you considered breaking up the 
code into functions?

You avoid a ton of duplicate code this way. Just turn anything that is repeated (or even 
that does something different) into a function. You don't need global variables either 
because you can just pass the variables as paramters from one function to the next.

I recommend to check out this book by Uncle Bob (he's also got a great video series) if 
you want some good techniques for writing code: https://www.amazon.com/Clean-Code-
Handbook-Software-Craftsmanship/dp/0132350882

The examples are in Java but the concepts are relevant to any coding language.

I have 2 questions about this week's lab. Here is a function from the solutions:


char *strcat_a(char dest[], char src[])
{
    char        *orig   = dest; //Is this equivalent to: char *orig = &dest[0] ?
    int         d=0, s=0;

    while(dest[d] != '\0')
    {
        ++d;
    }
    while(src[s] != '\0')
    {
        dest[d] = src[s];
        ++s;
        ++d;
    }
    dest[d]     = '\0';

    return orig;
}

1. Why don't we get an "array index out of bounds" error, since dest is an array 
(therefore has fixed length), and we go past the end of it by first going to the 
end of it, and then continually doing ++d ?

2. We return orig, which is a pointer to the first element of dest (which is a char 
array). Therefore, isn't this saying that the function returns the first letter of 
the final concatenated string? 



Date: Fri Oct  6 23:05:24 2017
To: help2002@csse.uwa.edu.au
Received: from 122.109.119.87
Subject: Lab 5
From: poster014@student.uwa.edu.au
X-smilie: none
X-img: none

ANONYMOUS wrote:

> I have 2 questions about this week's lab. Here is a function from the solutions:
>....
> 
> 1. Why don't we get an "array index out of bounds" error, since dest is an array 
> (therefore has fixed length), and we go past the end of it by first going to the 
> end of it, and then continually doing ++d ?

You are conflating the 'end of a string' with the 'end of an array' - they are not the
same thing.

If I declare and initialise an array as follows: 

    char temp[50];
    strcpy(temp,"Hello");

The '\0' character will be at temp[5], as it is the 6th element of the string "Hello".
However - the array is still 50 characters long in memory; we have room for 44 more
characters! The sample solution finds the null byte, which is the point at which we can
start concatenating the elements from the src array into the rest of the dest array. 

An index out of bounds error may occur if you attempt to copy from a src array that has
more elements than dest - but as a programmer, you should make sure you know what you're
copying into what! 

> 2. We return orig, which is a pointer to the first element of dest (which is a char 
> array). Therefore, isn't this saying that the function returns the first letter of 
> the final concatenated string? 

This is almost correct - we are returning a **pointer** to the first element of the
final concatenated string. You will find a lot of the time we do not assign the return
value of these pointers to anything.




Date: Sat Oct  7 07:17:36 2017
To: help2002@csse.uwa.edu.au
Received: from 106.68.222.189
Subject: Lab 5
From: chris.mcdonald@uwa.edu.au
X-smilie: none
X-img: none

Thanks Ryan.

ANONYMOUS wrote:

> 1. Why don't we get an "array index out of bounds" error, since dest is an array 
> (therefore has fixed length), and we go past the end of it by first going to the 
> end of it, and then continually doing ++d ?

Sort of paraphrasing Ryan's answer: the nature of the standard strcat() function - see man 
strcat - is that there must already be a string in the destination array, and we're 
appending a new string to the end of the original.

It's also assumed (required for correct operation) that the provided destination array is 
long enough to hold both the original string, the newly appended string, and a single null-
byte.  If we're concerned that the destination array might not be long enough, then we 
should use strncat() instead.

Date: Tue Oct 10 07:15:34 2017
To: help2002@csse.uwa.edu.au
Received: from 106.68.222.189
Subject: Header file in last week's lab
From: chris.mcdonald@uwa.edu.au
X-smilie: none
X-img: none

ANONYMOUS wrote:

> The header files weren't given in the solutions for the lab, but this is all I wrote 
> in my pointers.h file:

The function names, such as strcat_p() and strcat_a(), are not part of the C standard and, 
so, there's no standard header file for them.
So, yes, the declarations you've provided are correct ones to place in your own header file.


> Is this correct, or am I missing something? Also, do I need to #include various 
> standard libraries (e.g. stdbool.h, stdlib.h) in header files, since they were 
> included in the example shown in the lecture?

You only need to #include any of the standard C header files if you use their features.
For example, if your own code *doesn't* use a bool, then you don't need <stdbool.h>
In addition, if any of the standard C header files, themselves, require the use of a bool, 
then those header files will #include <stdbool.h> themselves.

And a note on terminology - they're called the standard header files; they are not the 
standard libraries, but declare things which are defined and implemented in the standard 
libraries.

Date: Tue Oct 10 18:44:42 2017

Previously, <chris.mcdonald@uwa.edu.au> wrote:

> The file contents look OK, provided those big indents are made with tabs.

Sorry, I spoke too quickly, while focusing on the 'clean' target.

The Makefile contents *don't* look OK - the way it's written, if any of the C source of header 
files are modified (more recently than that file st.o), then *all* of the C files will be 
recompiled.

The intention of using a Makefile is not simply to save typing, but to only undertaken the 
minimal effort (compilations) to rebuild the whole project.

Date: Wed Oct 11 05:25:46 2017
To: help2002@csse.uwa.edu.au
Received: from 106.68.222.189
Subject: Lab 5 - makefile
From: chris.mcdonald@uwa.edu.au
X-smilie: none
X-img: none

ANONYMOUS wrote:

> By the way, why do we delete the executable and the object files? I thought the point of the makefile was 
> so that you didn't have to make a new copy of the executable if nothing has changed. But if the executable 
> is deleted then we will have to make a new copy of it anyway. 

It's just a convenient (and accurate) way to cleanup up your directory of 'unwanted' files.
For example, I'll generally do a 'make clean' in a directory to reduce space before doing a backup, or saving the 
directory's important files to Dropbox, etc.
As we're using a Makefile, we can always create those files again, when needed.

Date: Fri Oct 13 14:34:12 2017

From: poster042@student.uwa.edu.au

I don't have time to go into the labs either because of work, and I own a Windows 10 PC. But assuming you have a 
PC with reasonable specs, you should be able to get Linux up and running within the hour, and for $0.00 cost.

Here are some different options:

1. Install Ubuntu Bash for Windows 10 (Ubuntu is a 'version' of Linux). This is the easiest option and it gives 
you Linux terminal that you can use directly from Windows OS. Read: https://www.howtogeek.com/249966/how-to-
install-and-use-the-linux-bash-shell-on-windows-10/

2. SSH into the UWA's iMacs and work from there (using bash, or Putty).

3. Install Ubuntu on a virtual machine on your PC. You can download the official Ubuntu image from 
https://www.ubuntu.com/ and a free VM from http://www.oracle.com/technetwork/server-
storage/virtualbox/downloads/index.html

4. Got a spare USB drive? You could actually use that to boot into a native Linux OS to work on. You could 
literally attach it to your house keys and carry it around in your pocket. A USB drive these days will cost you 
less than a free-range chicken sandwich.

The Linux environment is good enough to work on the project, but you'll need to test your solution on the school 
iMacs via SSH before submiting. The SSH instructions are on the CITS2002 page (iMacs via Internet).

Good luck!

'not available' and referred to the Faculty's Academic Conduct Advisor.
39 students received a penalty for a late submission.

- If your mark is recorded as 'not available' (18 students) you'll receive an email from me shortly.

- If your mark is recorded as 'not submitted' (38 students), and you believe that you made a project submission, please 
look for it in cssubmit (perhaps mistakenly in another unit?), or ask your project partner because they may have 
incorrectly recorded your student number - then email me with some details.

  *** WEDNESDAY 25th OCTOBER.

_____
Just to clarify - the automated section of the marking was performed by comparing the output of your project against 
the output of the sample solution (for which you now have the source code).  A subset of each of the provided sample 
test files was used.  

Before the comparison, any MAC addresses in your output were converted to lowercase, and all hyphens converted to 
colons.  Partial marks were awarded for comparisons that almost matched - if the outputs matched identically, you 
received full marks for that test;  if the outputs matched after all whitespace was removed you lost only 1 mark; if 
the outputs matched once it was sorted you lost only 1 mark.

If your project crashed (a signal terminated its execution) while running a particular test, or if your project took 
longer than 30seconds to complete any test, then you received no marks for that test.

____
And a big thanks to both Trent and Ryan, who did a great (and quick) job with the manual inspection
of the project submissions.

It's not wrong, if you are using a pipe and try to wait for the child to finish the pipe will fill up 
and the child will hang until the parent takes some output from the pipe. If the parent waits until 
the child closes the pipe (which it does) it guarantees the child was complete (well not entirely but  
it has no more output). In future I suggest being less blunt about something being wrong when you 
haven't understood the question properly.

Date: Wed Oct 18 16:35:32 2017
To: help2002@csse.uwa.edu.au
Received: from 144.136.102.225
Subject: Project 1 - Deducted marks for using pipe without wait
From: poster077@student.uwa.edu.au
X-smilie: none
X-img: none
X-anonymous: yes

Orphaned processes are automatically waited on by init. See below:

Zombie processes should not be confused with orphan processes: an orphan 
process is a process that is still executing, but whose parent has died. 
These do not remain as zombie processes; instead, (like all orphaned 
processes) they are adopted by init (process ID 1), which waits on its 
children. The result is that a process that is both a zombie and an orphan 
will be reaped automatically.

> c) you think Chris, Ryan and Trent are vindictive enough to take marks away for something correct, or not bad practice,
> d) you seem to think you know better than the aforementioned who have a lot of experience with this stuff, and that they believe in the impossible,
> or e) linear combinations of the above.

I believe this discussion has become counter-productive to the OP's intention. I do not think any more replies are necessary in this thread. 

In this scenario, piping was not an explicit requirement of the project; as a result, we were looking to see if students were using correct practises when calling sort 
via execv, and making sure they are waiting for the process to finish. This is 'good practise', as it is possible to write code that uses a child process and works 
perfectly well without having the wait() in there; this is perhaps confirmed by the number of submissions we had that worked well enough to pass people's tests, but 
did not call wait(&status). 

It is also - from my understanding - possible to use wait() with a pipe. It is also entirely possible to use pipes and NOT require wait(), based on the inherent 
functionality of pipes. However, because we are not able to spend an awful lot of time reading into the logic of the code (this ostensibly being marked on the ability 
for it to deliver the right results to the auto-run marker), it is entirely possible we have taken the absence of a wait() call at face value, and not internalised the 
context for why it has not been called in this scenario. 

It is always good practise - at least, in university projects - to follow the techniques and ideas that have been presented in the lecture material and labs. It was 
discussed in the lectures that calling wait() was necessary, and is good practise, so that should have been an indication that it is necessary to do so (within the 
context of this assignment). 

At the end of the day, if you feel as if you have been marked down unfairly, please let us know.

Date: Thu Oct 19 08:00:23 2017
To: help2002@csse.uwa.edu.au
Received: from 124.148.234.126
Subject: the provided files give an error when using make
From: chris.mcdonald@uwa.edu.au
X-smilie: none
X-img: none

(Annoyingly) we're observing that the (many) different Linux distributions being used have different 
header files provided by their C compilers - e.g. see the project clarification of Oct 15th.

If using Linux, change lines near the top of your myshell.h header file to read:

#if defined(__linux__)
    extern      char    *strdup(const char *str);
    extern      int     fileno(FILE *fp);
#endif

so that your code will be aware of the existence of fileno() as an external function.
Under Linux, it appears to be declared in some distributions, but not others.

Date: Thu Oct 19 08:10:36 2017
To: help2002@csse.uwa.edu.au
Received: from 124.148.234.126
Subject: Makefile not detecting std=c99
From: chris.mcdonald@uwa.edu.au
X-smilie: none
X-img: none

Resolved;  In case others see a similar problem -

the Makefile being developed *did not* have tab characters at the beginning of its 
command/action lines.

It *looked* like it did, because the text editor being used to edit the Makefile had been 
configured to replace a typed tab character with 4 space characters - something some editors 
to do to map between 'hard' and 'soft' tab characters to allow consistent indenting of 2, 4, 
6, .....  spaces.

And, just in case you don't watch the hilarious Silicon Valley:

  https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=V7PLxL8jIl8

Date: Thu Oct 19 13:11:23 2017
To: help2002@csse.uwa.edu.au
Received: from 124.148.234.126
Subject: Project 1 ".txt" Problem
From: chris.mcdonald@uwa.edu.au
X-smilie: none
X-img: none

And to re-inforce Ryan's comment - many of the projects that I am re-looking at, after students' 
concerned emails, have the problem where they either require, or assume that all input files have an 
extension of ".txt",  some projects also trying to first add ".txt" to files without it.

Often, these projects crash if the required or assumed extension does not exist.  The result is is 
typically:

- the program crashes and produces no output, or
- the program was only ever tested with files having the extension.

Date: Fri Oct 20 18:48:51 2017
To: help2002@csse.uwa.edu.au
Received: from 220.253.242.129
Subject: Makefile
From: chris.mcdonald@uwa.edu.au
X-smilie: none
X-img: none

ANONYMOUS wrote:

> how to fix this? I wanna type make instead adding -f and the filename. Thanks

The make program first looks for a file named 'makefile', then for one named 'Makefile', 
to locate the specification it will use (to build your project).

If you *choose* to use any other filename, then you can run

   make -f anyotherfilename

For some reason, you've named your specification file 'makefile.make' (why?) and so make 
will use it iff you tell it to.  Otherwise make will not find either of the two files it 
looks for,

From: poster098@student.uwa.edu.au

X-img: none

Whilst re-reading the lecture slides on dynamic memory allocation, I have a query. 
It says that malloc should be used when you don't know in advance how big your 
array needs to be, so they give this example code to illustrate:


char *my_strdup2(char *str)
{
    char *new = malloc( strlen(str) + 1 );

    if(new != NULL)
    {
        strcpy(new, str);   
    }
    return new;
}

That makes sense, but couldn't you just do this:


char *my_strdup2(char *str)
{
    int required_size = strlen(str) + 1;
    char *new[required_size];

    strcpy(new, str); 

    return new;
}

After doing some reading, I found that malloc is useful when you want your variable 
to actually exist AFTER the function has been executed...so is this the primary 
reason to use malloc?

The 'date' command does not recognise/accept the argument 'monday',
so you'd be expecting that command to fail and to print an error message.

You can check this, and other commands by trying it in your standard shell:

prompt> date monday
 ... error message
prompt> echo $status
1


When you say "because i am getting 0", you're probably getting it from the wrong place/way?
