Date: Mon Oct 23 05:57:34 2017
To: help2002@csse.uwa.edu.au
Received: from 124.148.221.130
Subject: The precedence of shell operators
From: chris.mcdonald@uwa.edu.au
X-smilie: none
X-img: none

Please take a copy of the new  parser.c  file for our project, from

   http://teaching.csse.uwa.edu.au/units/CITS2002/projects/parser.c

It is 11150 bytes long (and does not have a ".txt" extension!)
It is a 'drop-in' replacement for the older parser, and you should not
need to change the design or implementation of your existing work.

The starting materials and the web-based display program have been updated
to use this new parser.


-----------------------
Why?
As we know there are a number of shells available on Unix-based
platforms - including sh, bash, ksh, csh, tcsh, and zsh.  Each has a
similar syntax and operates similarly but, of course, the differences
are the very reason for having different shells.

Our area of discussion has been the precedence, and hence execution
order, of the operators '&&' and '||'.   As our project is wishing
to develop a subset of the features of the shell you use, sh or bash,
we'll follow its online manual entry:

    " A list is a sequence of one or more pipelines separated by one of
      the operators ;, &, &&, or ||, and optionally terminated by one of
      ;, &, or <newline>.
      Of these list operators, && and || have equal precedence, followed
      by ; and &, which have equal precedence.  "

Great, a simpler solution required than worrying about the precendence
of '&&' and '||',  and good to clear things up.

We can see some of the differences between shells, and test our own
project's similarity to sh or bash with a simple test program and
experimenting with the order of operators.  Consider the C99 program:

    #include <stdio.h>
    #include <stdlib.h>

    int main(int argc, char *argv[])
    {
        int status = (argv[0][0] == 's') ? EXIT_SUCCESS : EXIT_FAILURE;
        printf("%s\n", argv[0]);
        exit(status);
        return status;
    }

which simply prints its own name, and exits with success or failure
depending on the first character of its name. Make 6 copies of this program:

    cc -std=c99 -Wall -Werror -pedantic -o success1 filename.c
    cp success1 success2
    cp success1 success3
    cp success1 fail1
    cp success1 fail2
    cp success1 fail3

Most standard shells can execute commands provided as command-line arguments
(but not myshell).  Consider the execution of some commands under sh and csh:

    prompt>  sh  -c "./success1 || ./success2 && ./success3"
    ./success1
    ./success3
    prompt>  csh -c "./success1 || ./success2 && ./success3"
    ./success1

    prompt>  sh  -c "./success1 || ./fail2 && ./success3"
    ./success1
    ./success3
    prompt>  csh -c "./success1 || ./fail2 && ./success3"
    ./success1

Same commands, different results - but both are 'correct' because that's how
they're designed to perform.

Why was I confused about whether our standard shells have difference
precedence for '&&' and '||', considering them equivalent to the
Boolean operators of 'and' and 'or'?  I teach another Masters unit,
CITS4407 Open Source Tools and Scripting, where we look at shells in detail,
particularly their use as programming languages.  sh and bash support
other Boolean operators (not required for our project!),  requested by using
the operators  -a  and  -o  in tests, which *do* follow the expected Boolean
order of execution (yes, even in the same shell!):

    if [ expression1 -a expression2 -o expression3 ]    # etc....

Welcome to systems programming, and good luck remembering all the differences :-)

Date: Mon Oct 23 10:06:54 2017
To: help2002@csse.uwa.edu.au
Received: from 130.95.1.70
Subject: Decisions on concerns about 1st project marking
From: chris.mcdonald@uwa.edu.au
X-smilie: none
X-img: none
X-announcefor: 5d

If your 1st project submission:

  - assumed that the input files ended in ".txt",
  - required that the input files ended in ".txt",
  - renamed the provided input files so that they ended in ".txt"

then, I'm afraid, that your approach to using a program's specified input
files was incorrect.  If your approach to any of these points resulted
in your project not producing the correct output, then you will (have)
lost marks for it.

There is no wording or sample code in lecture notes, laboratory
sheets and solutions, or workshop questions and solutions, that
text files must have the ".txt" extension.  While the sample project
solution still works if you provide input filenames with the ".txt"
extension, it does not require or modify filenames to enforce it.
Moreover, the topic was discussed in over 25 articles on our help
forum while the project was running.

I have reviewed all 184 submissions, and only 21 have (clearly)
taken this approach, so I do not believe that it was a systemic
misunderstanding.  However, based on emails that I have received,
it appears that a number of other students have misunderstood the
issue.  If your project created a new (text) file holding data to
be passed to the sort program, and you gave the file an extension
of ".txt", then that is not a problem, and you lost no marks for that.

I will revisit the project submission in which the above error may
have resulted in your losing marks for multiple tests, and contact
you by email.

_______
Another point of concern was the requirement that projects used the
system call wait() to wait for the termination of child processes.
This had nothing to do with whether your project used pipes or
temporary text files, as the wait() call was simply required to
wait for the termination of the sort program.  The markers were not
looking to see if you used wait() to determine if all output back
from sort must have been sent.  The use of wait() has nothing to
do with enforcing any particular scheduling pattern, nor directing
what happens if a pipe fills with data before it is read.  Yes,
processes which lose their parents are inherited by the init process,
but it's strongly preferred practice to wait for a child process,
rather than to just 'forget' about it.  It's akin to using fclose()
to close a file, if our code used fopen() to open it.

_______
I do not wish to be adversarial on the above points, and wish to remind
you that you're still welcome to formally appeal this point through
the Faculty's Student Office:
  http://www.governance.uwa.edu.au/procedures/policies/policies-and-procedures?method=document&id=UP15%2F1

Date: Tue Oct 24 11:50:31 2017
To: help2002@csse.uwa.edu.au
Received: from 130.95.1.70
Subject: Step 1 - Commands
From: chris.mcdonald@uwa.edu.au
X-smilie: none
X-img: none

Eugene's answer is a good one.

(you may have some confusion,here)  but the shell does not *implement* the several thousand 
commands that we (could) use daily - it simply finds them and (attempts) to execute them.

This is a good approach because, if we write or download+install a new command, then the shells 
do not need to be informed or recompiled - they just need to find and execute the new command.

Date: Tue Oct 24 11:52:24 2017
To: help2002@csse.uwa.edu.au
Received: from 130.95.1.70
Subject: Subshell clarification
From: chris.mcdonald@uwa.edu.au
X-smilie: none
X-img: none

Yes. The construction of the SHELLCMD tree is deterministic, no randomness.

Date: Tue Oct 24 13:25:41 2017
To: help2002@csse.uwa.edu.au
Received: from 130.95.1.70
Subject: Contiguous memory
From: chris.mcdonald@uwa.edu.au
X-smilie: none
X-img: none

ANONYMOUS wrote:

> I've read that malloc/calloc/realloc gives a block of contiguous memory. Does this 
> mean it gives n bytes of memory that have contiguous LOGICAL addresses or contiguous 
> PHYSICAL addreses?

Logical.

 
> Also, more generally, when we create an array, e.g. int array[] = {1,2,3,4,5} are the 
> elements stored contiguously (physically or logically)?

Yes, again logically.
It's only when our programs request access to a logical address, that the hardware memory 
management unit determines on which physical page or segment the required data or instruction 
really is.

Date: Wed Oct 25 05:14:47 2017
To: help2002@csse.uwa.edu.au
Received: from 124.148.221.130
Subject: IO redirection clarification
From: chris.mcdonald@uwa.edu.au
X-smilie: none
X-img: none

ANONYMOUS wrote:

> > No, the cd command does not read its destination directory from its standard input.
> 
>   So the contents of the file should not be read and executed? Or does the shell only read the 
>   contents of the file as it streams in?
>   
>   This is from the specification page:
>   "The sequence   command < infile   requests that the command use infile as its standard input."

The sentence you've quoted is correct.  The contents of 'infile' provides the standard input to any general 
command, including cd, replacing the keyboard for just that command.
However, the cd command does not require or read any additional input to do its work, and will not actually 
read anything from its standard input.

>   How does a command have a file as it's standard input?

The file redirection provides the names of files from which a command's input may be read (or written to).
Using both input and output redirection, we can copy one file to another with:

   cat < oldfile > newfile

Commands use a variety of mechanisms, and combinations of them, to work in different ways - including 
requiring no, a fixed, or a variable number of command-line arguments, input file redirection, and output 
redirection.

Date: Wed Oct 25 16:08:16 2017
To: help2002@csse.uwa.edu.au

Subject: IO redirection clarification
From: poster096@student.uwa.edu.au
X-smilie: none
X-img: none
X-anonymous: yes

I think you might be confusing command line arguments with standard input. The command cd takes a directory name as an 
argument.

So you would call it like this:

cd [directory_name]

Now consider the program wc (word count). It can take a file name as a command line argument, like so:

wc [my_file]

But if you do not provide a file on the command line, it will read from standard input, which is by default the keyboard 
(displayed through the terminal). So if you just call "wc", the terminal will prompt you to write some stuff, and you can 
stop by calling Cntrl-d. wc will count how many words/lines/characters you wrote. Try it.

When the specs say "The sequence   command < infile   requests that the command use infile as its standard input." It 
means that instead of waiting for you to write some stuff on the keyboard, the program will go ahead and process whatever 
is in the file.

So if you call "wc < infile", wc will count the words/lines/characters in infile. Note that this is exactly the same as 
calling "wc infile". This is because wc is a 'filter' - it takes a file as an argument or reads from standard input, and 
'<' means to use the file as standard input.

TL;DR: command line arguments are not the same as standard input. Arguments tell the program how to behave, whereas 
standard input is what the program actually processes.

Date: Wed Oct 25 19:23:53 2017
To: help2002@csse.uwa.edu.au
Received: from 121.200.37.184
Subject: Do programs read input from stdin or file descriptor 0?
From: poster029@student.uwa.edu.au
X-smilie: none
X-img: none

A program will read its input from stdin and send its output to stdout.

stdin, stderr and stdout are all created by the C runtime at program startup, and each have an 
associated file descriptor.

You shouldn't rely on stdin or stdout mapping to specific file descriptor numbers, as this is an 
implementation detail. Instead, use STDIN_FILENO, STDOUT_FILENO, etc.

The trick to step 6 is to make the standard input/output file descriptors map to the infile/outfile 
somehow. For this you should investigate the dup2 function.

Good luck with the project.

> I have read the previous post regarding this too but am still confused. 
> Thanks

That would just indicate that cal interprets standard input in that way, not that it uses 
them as command line arguments.
Think of sort as an example. Sort < infile does the same as sort infile, because as sort 
infile (infile as a command line arg) sorts the contense of a file, but sort with no args 
sorts standard input, so if you have a file that says "hi bob my name is jeff" and go sort < 
infile it'll sort that, (for comparison also try sort with no args on bash, type the same 
text, and press ctrd D to end stdin), not use "hi bob my name is jeff" as command line 
arguments which tell the command how to run.
For cal, I don't know for sure, but I think if you tried using your infile as -y 2014 or 
some other cal flags you could normally use as arguments maybe it would get confused (?), 
but I really don't know for sure.
Hope that kind of made sense?

Date: Thu Oct 26 23:10:12 2017
To: help2002@csse.uwa.edu.au
Received: from 122.109.114.35
Subject: Pipeline
From: poster080@student.uwa.edu.au
X-smilie: none
X-img: none
X-anonymous: yes

Ok so there is a way to use wait with the pipe, you just want to do  it at the right time, because you want to allow 
your child processes to complete before exiting or carrying on with your parent process. If you're getting hangups 
and havent used wait the issue is something else though.

Be careful also with where you're closing pipe ends. Also the guy mentioning abrupt exits, you might want to consider 
the logic of what you're doing to file descriptors and how you might need to change it (hint: look at myshell.c and 
one of its while loops!).

Honestly, I would suspect you both might have issues mainly with how/where you're forking and closing file 
descriptors and stuff. I really don't want to risk giving anything too much away about the nitty gritty so won't say 
more than consider that you want to be able to do the following: link up stdin/stdout to a pipe, do stuff relating to 
stdio, and close the pipe... (think about the consequences of each part!)

Execv can't do anything but a program and arguments, so saying you would 
execv the program and infile is the same as using infile as a command line argument,
since execv just takes a program and arguments, which as previously stated 
is not at all the same as standard input.

Previously, <poster049@student.uwa.edu.au> wrote:

> I must say this has us stumped too.
> 
> What kinds of commands take an infile?
> 
> I had thought that the infile contained arguments to be used by the command. 
> Ie running - 
> cal < infile
> 
> and the infile contains - 
> -y 2017
> 
> Then the shell would run - 
> cal -y 2017
> 
> This however doesn't seem to be how it works.  I can see that sort < infile sorts the file, but what 
> other commands require this kind of operation?
> 
> Thanks

Just completely separate infile and command line arguments in your head. Completely. They're not the 
same.

Infile is used as standard input. That is it. Some commands and programs work with standard in, and how 
they work with standard in is defined in the command or program.

Arguments are different, they tell the program what to do with their input.

The specifics of which commands require it is irrelevant, what matters is that you can make a command 
that has an option for standard in to work in some way run using standard in from a file... instead of 
from your keyboard and mouse.

> Do we need individual files for each step in order to not lose marks? At present, our

There is no set rule on how you split it up - a good rule of thumb is to split up independent 
pieces of program logic. One file per step is a good start, but if you feel like you can have a 
couple of questions in the same file and the code is still "clean", then that should not be an 
issue. We just don't want to see monolithic files. :)

Just to let you know, there is nothing inherently wrong with a file that has a "small" amount of code - 
whatever the boundary conditions may be for a "small" classification. If separating the program into 
smaller segments helps readability, that is a good thing. 

You will not be penalised for having a file with a single function that does exactly what the question 
asks you for, and uses correct C programming practises and is well commented. 

I am not telling you what you are doing is wrong - just letting others know that if they prefer to keep 
things separate, they are more than welcome to do so. :)

	char *ptr = &chapter;
	int numSentences = 0;
	int *nSentences = &numSentences;
	while(*ptr != '\0'){
		//EXTRACT A SENTENCE:
		int sentenceLength = 0;
		char *line = malloc(sizeof(char));
		while(*ptr != '.'){
			*line = *ptr;
			sentenceLength++;
			line = realloc(n*sizeof(char));
			line++;
			ptr++;
		}
		//**ADD THE SENTENCE TO AN ARRAY OF SENTENCES**
		*nSentences++;
	}
	//RETURN THE ARRAY OF SENTENCES
}

> // Closes file (input)
> 
> char file_input[1000]; // This file has every character in the file
> char *const argv[3] = {ORIGINAL COMMAND (e.g. sort), file_input, NULL};
> execv(argv[0], argv);
> 
> I have altered this code for quite a while but I am quite stuck at this point. Any help is 
> appreciated. =)

I think you are misunderstanding the nature of stdin/stdout. You should not need to read in the 
file into an array to redirect the output.

Date: Sat Oct 28 16:46:26 2017
To: help2002@csse.uwa.edu.au

Subject: File Redirection - Coding In Circles
From: poster080@student.uwa.edu.au

X-img: none
X-anonymous: yes

ANONYMOUS wrote:

> Previously, <poster014@student.uwa.edu.au> wrote:
> 
> > I think you are misunderstanding the nature of stdin/stdout. You should not need to read in the 
> > file into an array to redirect the output. 
> 
> Ah okay, however, I don't quite get how we use can use execv() to include both t->argv for the original 
> command and the new standard input after t has been created. For example, sort test.txt works due to step 2, 
> but I don't quite know how to make sort < test.txt work in step 6.

execv doesn't include the "new standard input", it automatically will use standard input, all you have to do is
make standard input what you want it to be. read manual pages for dup2 and open(2) properly, and revisit the slides
from the lecture where file descriptors were talked about. just as a note, when you put in cmd1 < infile, the argv
from the SHELLCMD relating to cmd1 won't have "<" and "infile" in it. It'll just have "cmd1", and t->infile will be infile.

Chris may upload the solutions at some stage, but I doubt it will before semester 
finishes. At any rate, it is much better to go through the questions without solutions 
first. 

 From experience talking to people practising for the mid-semester test, a lot of people 
did not attempt to answer some of the code-related questions by simply trying them out 
on the computer - we have the luxury in computer science of being able to do that, and 
learn a lot as a result. This may help direct your exam study if you are currently 
without direction. 

With respect to scaling, that is dependent on a range of factors - the biggest factor, 
of course, being your (and your peer's) performance. I am going to be *that guy* and 
suggest working towards getting as good a mark as possible, without factoring in 
scaling. Especially when there is no guarantee that, if marks *are* scaled, they will be 
scaled UP...

Ryan [lab guy]

Date: Mon Oct 30 09:13:09 2017
To: help2002@csse.uwa.edu.au
Received: from 120.16.110.173
Subject: Stdio for background processes
From: chris.mcdonald@uwa.edu.au
X-smilie: none
X-img: none

As you've stated, many standard programs anticipate having their standard input open, even 
if they don't require any input or can work with 'no' input.

The general practice is to open the special file  /dev/null
to a background process's standard input - there's a connected file there, and as soon as 
you read from it you get the end-of-file condition.

Date: Mon Oct 30 09:17:37 2017
To: help2002@csse.uwa.edu.au
Received: from 120.16.110.173
Subject: Step 3 External commands
From: chris.mcdonald@uwa.edu.au
X-smilie: none
X-img: none

ANONYMOUS wrote:

> Do these external commands and their behaviour apply when part of a tree like 
> 
> myshell.1 cmd1 ; cmd2 || cd ; time cal
> 
> or is it just for single inputs in the shell like
> 
> myshell.1 cd
> 
> myshell.1 time cal

*All* commands that you shell can execute, except for the 3 required *internal* commands 
specified in the project sheet are termed *external commands*,
and they can occur anywhere in command sequences.

You can find other posts that you've made before by clicking on your own square icon or photo.

 
> Not sure if other people are having this problem, but I can't seem to get CDPATH to show up 
> in the environment variables, both on bash on ubuntu on Windows, as well as the CSSE Macs. 
> I know the myshell.c automatically initialises CDPATH to ../.. if detects the environment 
> variable as NULL. But because of this, I don't know how I would go about checking the 
> functionality with CDPATH for both the cd and shellscript commands.

CDPATH is usually an environment variable used internally by the shell;
I do not know if it's exported from your shell, on all platforms, to then be available to other 
programs.  If not (on Windows?) type:

     export CDPATH="dir1:dir2:dir3"

in your standard shell.

Date: Mon Oct 30 09:42:02 2017
To: help2002@csse.uwa.edu.au
Received: from 120.16.110.173
Subject: Exit Status confirmation
From: chris.mcdonald@uwa.edu.au
X-smilie: none
X-img: none

Previously, <poster049@student.uwa.edu.au> wrote:

> When using chdir() the value on fail is -1 which is not a valid exit status (range 0-255)
> 
> Should the exit status in this case be EXIT_FAILURE or should it be set to errno for this 
> process?

You may be confusing the internal and external ways of indicating errors.
If the call to the chdir() function fails, with -1 as you say, and you need this reflected output 
side of myshell (when it exits) then, yes, report it as EXIT_FAILURE.

errno is a variable only seen/known within a running program; it's final value is not 
reflected/known by external programs.

Date: Mon Oct 30 17:11:28 2017
To: help2002@csse.uwa.edu.au
Received: from 120.16.110.173
Subject: Step 7 - Subshell in Pipelines 
From: chris.mcdonald@uwa.edu.au
X-smilie: none
X-img: none

Previously, <poster085@student.uwa.edu.au> wrote:

> Thanks! I guess what I want it to test is the correct flow/result of the command (and clear my thoughts).

Yes, always the right approach.
Keep in mind that your standard shell will also produce the correct result (and the sample solution is supposed 
to!).

With examples like   ls | (wc | wc)

you can see the progression of data, left to right, by running 'by hand' each of:

ls
ls | wc
ls | wc | wc
ls | (wc | wc)

and ensuring that it's giving the results expected.

Then, for more a test of your logic about subshells, than implementation, try:

  (cd /tmp ; ls) ; ls

Date: Tue Oct 31 12:53:01 2017
To: help2002@csse.uwa.edu.au
Received: from 130.95.108.170
Subject: myshell not prompting for next command after file redirection
From: poster096@student.uwa.edu.au
X-smilie: none
X-img: none
X-anonymous: yes

ANONYMOUS wrote:

> So far, file redirection is working as expected, but the prompt for the next 
> command does not appear after the fact.
> 
> This is what I'm getting:
> 
> myshell.1 ls > output.txt
> 
> As in, myshell.2 does not appear. Additionally the output.txt file reads as 
> follows:
> 
> ---
> 
> desktop.ini
> execute.c
> execute.o
> globals.c
> globals.o
> makefile
> myshell
> myshell.c
> myshell.h
> myshellnew
> myshell.o
> output.txt
> parser.c
> parser.o
> 
> myshell.2
> 
> ---
> 
> It looks like the next prompt got recorded in the file somehow.
> 
> Any ideas what could be causing this issue? Thanks!

The file descriptor table is per-process, not local to a function or block of code. 
Ie, if we redirect standard IO a file, the process will continue to send output 
there until its termination or until standard IO is redirected somewhere else again. 

So somewhere you have redirected the standard output to output.txt. But for which 
process did you redirect standard output? Hint: myshell never actually redirects its 
output anywhere.


Date: Tue Oct 31 14:43:36 2017
To: help2002@csse.uwa.edu.au

Subject: Multiple Output Redirection
From: chris.mcdonald@uwa.edu.au

X-img: none

ANONYMOUS wrote:

> Bash supports the following "type" of commands:
> 
>    ls > output1.txt > output2.txt

Not required; we're only building a small subset of a 'real' shell.
No need to imagine it any more difficult than it is :-)

As an aside,  (ls > output1.txt) > output2.txt

is a valid command sequence, with the output of ls appearing in the first file, and 
nothing appearing in the second (a zero-sized file should be created).

ANONYMOUS wrote:

> I'm slightly worried that I have the wrong approach to Step 8 as I didn't require fopen() 
> or fclose(), because I was implementing:

There's no requirement to use any or all of the suggested functions; they're hints and/but there's 
often a few different ways to implement each step.

> 1. Check if command-line input is of the form ./executable_file.

Why are you checking for that form?
The presence of a '/' anywhere in the command's name indicates if the value of PATH should be used, 
independent of whether it turns out to be an executable (a binary, .exe...) or a shellscript.

There's no special forms to look for.


> It doesn't work perfectly yet - it only reads and executes the first line of the text file 
> - but I was wondering if my interpretation of the problem was wrong and what I have missed.

Did you read the responses to your other thread, about that 1st line problem:

  https://secure.csse.uwa.edu.au/run/help2002?p=np&a=570&all=y4
?

No :-)
Thanks for the detailed description (thought I didn't think I'd get this simple thing wrong :-)

While to both you and I running just   man
with no arguments looks like it should be an error (and hence man should exit with failure) it 
appears that 'man' on macOS exits with a 0, and on Linux exits with a 1.
Neither manual entry mentions what is expected in this situation.

So the myshell sample is correct because it's just reflecting what its host operating system 
and application programs tell it.

If you just run commands, myshell shouldnt exit. Sleep5&cal should do cal, and in the background sleep, and go to the next input line "myshell.2" or whatever number while sleep still runs, it shouldn't exit.
The idea is you could run some process that takes a long time, keep doing stuff, and it goes in the background, but if you exit with exit then it should terminate the background process(es) thats(/'re) still going if there are any

Date: Thu Nov  2 09:11:49 2017
To: help2002@csse.uwa.edu.au
Received: from 120.16.110.173
Subject: Background process not continuing to prompt
From: chris.mcdonald@uwa.edu.au
X-smilie: none
X-img: none

Previously, <poster049@student.uwa.edu.au> wrote:

> If we enter sleep 5 & ls into our myshell it runs ls and then sleeps for the 5 but doesn't 
> give a my.shell prompt in between.

Unsure how to diagnose what you're seeing, but it's not what you want (you know that!)

The only thing you should 'see' is the output of ls followed by the next prompt.
You should not 'see' any sleeping as that's happening in the background and (initially) you don't 
care about it, and so won't be waiting for it.

Should be no need to use kill to stop and restart any process. 

Date: Thu Nov  2 09:15:02 2017
To: help2002@csse.uwa.edu.au

Subject: Shellscript loops
From: chris.mcdonald@uwa.edu.au
X-smilie: none
X-img: none

Previously, <poster080@student.uwa.edu.au> wrote:

> just wondering if we're to assume shellscripts will at most make use of the ; || && | & and < > >> operators in command sequences?

Shellscripts are just text files containing sequences of other valid/understood commands - even calls to other shellscripts.

> No loops or control sequences of commands?

For this project we're not implementing any loops (would require changes to the parser, and it's not one of the listed Steps), but the 
script can contain the supported command sequences.

Subject: Handling background process termination signals
From: poster026@student.uwa.edu.au
X-smilie: none
X-img: none
X-anonymous: yes

When a background process is forked from the parent, but not waited on for
completion, the parent should be informed of the completion by catching a
termination signal with a user defined signal handler. Is this correct?

The problem is that once the handler is 'installed' it then catches the termination
signal of every child process, not just the one that was put in the background. So
you have to reap every child in the handler, rather than just have the kernel ignore
the signal by default. 

If what I've said above is correct then must myshell keep track of all the process
id's (and/or process group id's) that are spawned from a fork in order to check, in
the signal handler, that the one that was just reaped is a background job so that
myshell may 'report which background process has terminated'?

This just doesn't seem, to me, to be the correct way to do it. Any hint's, tips or
advice would be really really helpful as I've been stuck on this for ages... not to
mention the deadline is fast approaching!

TL;DR
Do we need to keep track of process id's (or at least background pid's)?

Cheers.

> TL;DR
> Do we need to keep track of process id's (or at least background pid's)?

Your myshell will only be informed about the termination of processes that it has created, 
and they are either background processes or 'others' - so why track both types?

Date: Thu Nov  2 11:36:28 2017

Subject: Submitting your 2nd project
From: chris.mcdonald@uwa.edu.au
X-smilie: none
X-img: none
X-announcefor: 1w


1) ensure that your name and student number (for 1 or 2 people)
   appears near the top of at least one file.
2) submit your Makefile, it's one of the marked steps
3) submit all the C and header files you've been working with -
   even any unchanged files such as parser.c
4) do not submit your own 'extra' files, such as shellscripts
5) ensure that your project compiles successfully on macOS

Please note, don't hang your hopes of a reply on help2002 on Friday morning.
Given that our final exam is very early, it's extremely unlikely that you'll
have your 2nd project marks back before then.

Good luck,

this may be happening? Also, apart from whatever's causing that error, does my 
solution look correct? Thank you!!


void countEntries(char *dirName, int *nFiles, int *nDirs){
    DIR *dirp;
    struct dirent *dp;
    dirp = opendir(dirName);
    if(dirp ==NULL){
    	perror("Can't open directory"); 
    	exit(EXIT_FAILURE);
    }
    while ((dp = readdir(dirp)) != NULL)
    {
    	if(dp -> d_type == DT_REG){
    		*nFiles++;
    		printf("%s\n", dp -> d_name);
    	}
    	if(dp -> d_type == DT_DIR){
    		*nDirs++;
        	countEntries(dp -> d_name, nFiles, nDirs);
    	}
    }
    closedir(dirp);
}

Date: Thu Nov  2 15:44:47 2017
To: help2002@csse.uwa.edu.au
Received: from 120.16.110.173
Subject: step 7 (pipes) - shell exiting after successful execution
From: chris.mcdonald@uwa.edu.au
X-smilie: none
X-img: none

ANONYMOUS wrote:

> If it helps diagnose the issue, I have used a fork to execute the left subtree command 
> within the child to assure the left hand of the pipe is completed before the right hand 
> command is (which the parent waits for the child completion, and then executes the right 
> shell command).

Do not do this.
You do not need to 'get between' two processes connected by a pipe, the shell plays no role in 
pushing the output through or interfering with the scheduling.

The left-hand-side could send no data through the pipe, or an 'infinite' amount, and then may 
terminate immediately or continue its execution 'indefinitely'.
The shell is unaware of how these commands execute or communicate, and should not be involved 
other than waiting for the right-hand-side to finish.

Date: Thu Nov  2 16:27:39 2017
To: help2002@csse.uwa.edu.au
Received: from 120.16.110.173
Subject: step 7 (pipes) - shell exiting after successful execution
From: chris.mcdonald@uwa.edu.au
X-smilie: none
X-img: none

ANONYMOUS wrote:

> I see so there is no need to use fork() here at all?

Too difficult to answer multiple anonymous people each describing their specific, though 
similar, problems.

We fork a process if we need a copy of it, and we need two concurrently executing 
processes if they are to communicate using a pipe  (you knew all this).
But the shell has no role other than creating and connecting these processes, and 
waiting for the 'most right-hand' process to terminate (by itself).

for the next command.  Outputs from different commands are not synchronised.

void countEntries(char* dirName, int *nFiles, int *nDirs){
    printf("%s\n", dirName);    
    DIR *dirp;
    struct dirent *dp;
    dirp = opendir(dirName);
    if(dirp ==NULL){
    	perror("Can't open directory"); 
    	exit(EXIT_FAILURE);
    }
    while ((dp = readdir(dirp)) != NULL)
    {
    	if(dp -> d_type == DT_REG){
    		*nFiles++;
    		printf("%s\n", dp -> d_name);
    	}
    	if(dp -> d_type == DT_DIR){
    		*nDirs++;
        	countEntries(dp -> d_name, nFiles, nDirs);
    	}
    }
    closedir(dirp);
}

Ok, I feel like I'm getting close now:


void countEntries(char* dirName, int *nFiles, int *nDirs){
    DIR *dirp;
    struct dirent *dp;
    dirp = opendir(dirName);
    if(dirp ==NULL){
    	perror("Can't open directory"); 
    	exit(EXIT_FAILURE);
    }
    while ((dp = readdir(dirp)) != NULL)
    {
    	if(dp -> d_type == DT_REG){
    		*nFiles++;
    		printf("%s\n", dp -> d_name);
    	}
    	if(dp -> d_type == DT_DIR){
    		*nDirs++;
    		char path[1024];
    		if(strcmp(dp->d_name, ".")==0 || strcmp(dp->d_name,"..")==0){
    			continue;
    		}
    		snprintf(path,sizeof(path),"%s/%s", dirName, dp -> d_name);
        	countEntries(path, nFiles, nDirs);
    	}
    }
    closedir(dirp);
}


int main(int argc, char *argv[]){
    int *ptr1 = malloc(sizeof(int));
    int *ptr2 = malloc(sizeof(int));
    countEntries(argv[1], ptr1, ptr2);
    printf("number of files = %i\n", *ptr1);
    printf("number of directories = %i\n",*ptr2);
	
    exit(EXIT_SUCCESS);
}

Here's what I've got now. I believe that I'm correctly extracting each sentence, but maybe going wrong

char **makeSentences(char *chapter, int *nSentences){
	char *ptr = chapter;
	int numSentences = 0;
	char **sentences = malloc(sizeof(char*));
	while(*ptr != '\0'){

		//EXTRACT A SENTENCE:

		int sentenceLength = 0;
		char *line = malloc(sizeof(char));
		while(*ptr != '.'){
			*line = *ptr;
			sentenceLength++;
			line = (char*)realloc(line, sentenceLength*sizeof(char));
			line++;
			ptr++;
		}

		//ADD THE SENTENCE TO AN ARRAY OF SENTENCES

		*sentences = line;
		numSentences++;
		sentences = realloc(sentences, numSentences * sizeof(char*));
		sentences++;
		
	}

	//RETURN THE ARRAY OF SENTENCES

	*nSentences = numSentences;
	return sentences;
}

Previously, <chris.mcdonald@uwa.edu.au> wrote:

> 
> > Firstly, why do I need the 
> > if(strcmp(dp->d_name, ".")==0 || strcmp(dp->d_name,"..")==0){continue;} 
> > part? I found this code snippet whilst reading online, but not quite sure why we'd 
> > get a "." (present directory) or ".." (parent directory) when going through our 
> > directory..?
> 
> Every directory contains a . and a .. entry (always the first two entries that can never be 
> removed).  Why do you think they're there,  and what would (does) happen if you make a 
> recursive call to either . or ..  ?

My understanding is that the . entry is so that you can tell which directory you are in, and the 
.. entry is an easy way of finding out what the parent directory is. If you recursively searched 
through the . or .. entries, then you would be stuck in an infinite loop.

ANONYMOUS wrote:

> Just wanna ask, 
> the question states:
> 
> "the function should match the following prototype: char **makeSentences(char *chapter, int 
> *nSentences);"
> 
> does this imply that int *nSentences is an input and we assumed it is given to us?

It's not exactly clear what *you* mean by 'input parameter' - C doesn't specifically use that 
terminology because parameters that, themselves, are pointers could (but don't have to be) used as 
input, output, or both  (using the common meaning from other languages).

However, the question asks "The number of sentences is returned via the function’s second parameter 
(nSentences)."  If the number of sentences was already known by the calling function, then there's 
be no reason to return that value, and so *nSentences would only be an input parameter.

??

Date: Sun Nov  5 22:55:34 2017

Previously, <poster001@student.uwa.edu.au> wrote:

> So, this is kind of a 'why didn't I ask this before the project'...
> 
> Lets say you want to use the university computers and vim to program. Vim's great but 
> it's missing a step through debugger. I was wondering what possible solutions there 
> were. Would you please provide a recommendation or maybe provide your preferred method 
> for coding c/c++?
> 
> Thanks.

Command line debugging is possible through gdb or lldb, which are command line debuggers. It 
is also possible to program in an editor - such as Vim, or Sublime/Atom etc. - and then 
debug in a GUI, such as CLion or DDD etc. This becomes more of a preference that will 
develop over time and through exposure to different tools. I'd give gdb/lldb (depending on 
whether you're compiling with gcc or clang) a go - it is a useful skill to have, especially 
when debugging embedded systems' code.

Date: Mon Nov  6 10:41:17 2017

ANONYMOUS wrote:

> So has anyone got any advice about whether my function is close to correct, and if so, where am I 
> going wrong? Cheers.

Unfortunately, close in the approach taken but some errors that will make it fail.

Consider that chapter will be a vector of sentences - chapter[0] will point to the first sentence, 
chapter[1] to the second....

You'll need to allocate/extend the space for chapter each time you find a new sentences, and then make the 
newly allocated element of chapter point to a copy of the recently found sentence.

Your approach seems too complicated - 2 mallocs and 2 reallocs; you may find it simpler with 1 realloc and 
1 strdup.

Sorry, you're correct - after the hyphen, substitute chapter[x] for sentence[x]
(typing on a phone, for the inexperienced, is not fun)


> I feel like this is what I'm doing, with this code:
> 
> 
> *sentences = line;
> numSentences++;
> sentences = realloc(sentences, numSentences * sizeof(char*));
> sentences++;

In the code, above, you are not copying the actual sentence's contents (you may be thinking that that first 
assignment does that?)

And you cannot increment the value of sentences, in the last line, if you're hoping to pass it to a future call of 
realloc().

are 'exposed' through the header files.  However, you may have more luck looking *inside* the header files

Certainly not #import,  as it's not a C pre-processor directive.  #include

The question you wish to ask is, however, not "do we have to...?" but "should we...?"

As discussed in the lecture/workshop discussing the final exam, YES, provide relevant #include 
statements to demonstrate your understanding.  Markers will assume very little about what you may 
know - provide evidence.

I think perhaps you are overcomplicating the question. Given it is asking you to create a data 
structure, think about how you could use other aspects of the C programming language you have 
learnt this year (e.g. structs) to work this out. 

I would also argue that a triple pointer is no more scary a concept than a double pointer. In 
fact, 'triple pointers' are not really a concept of their own; rather, it is just an instance 
of the pointer concept, in which a variable is pointing to a space in memory that itself is 
pointing to a space in memory. In these instances, a diagram can be very helpful - something I 
still do to this day if I'm having difficulty navigating a code base.



Date: Wed Nov  8 09:50:08 2017

ANONYMOUS wrote:

> I think the most challenging aspect is the fact that we have to use triple pointers 
> (at least I think we do), and in class, we've only really used single pointers (apart 
> from a few occasions maybe). I think that it wouldn't be too difficult to do if you 
> had around 30 minutes and a computer to test your methodology (and search online for 
> helpful functions), but doing it in around 20mins with no computer is very very 
> daunting for me (and others, I'm assuming). The fact that we have to use triple 
> pointers (which is a brand new concept for majority of the class) makes it even 
> worse.

OK, as Ryan has suggested, let's try to simplify the apparent complexity of the problem.

Our spreadsheet is a 2D array of strings so, yes, using a triple pointer in summary.
What if, instead of each cell being s string (a pointer to a character), each cell was just an 
integer - we'd then have just a 2D array of integers, which seems simpler.

And if it were cells of integers, then we'd likely use simple arithmetic and assignment 
operators to set the cell's values,  as in

   cell[r][c] = newvalue;
   printf("%i ", cell[r][c] );
   ....
   cell[r][c] = 0;

Remembering that they're cells of strings, we can treat these assignment operators using simple 
operations/functions rather than ever having to deal with that 3rd level of pointer:

   cell[r][c] = strdup(newvalue);
   printf("%s ", cell[r][c] );
   ....
   free( cell[r][c] );
   cell[r][c] = NULL;

_____
Does that start to look any simpler?

That does seem to help. Cheers.

I've scratched up a draft solution:



//Define a C99 data structure to define an instance of the 2-dimensional spreadsheet
char*** spreadsheet;

______________________________________________________________________________________________


//function to return a pointer to allocated memory suitable for storing/managing spreadsheet

char*** return_spreadsheet(int nRows, int nCols){
	spreadsheet = malloc(sizeof(char*)*nRows*nCols);
	return spreadsheet;
}

______________________________________________________________________________________________


//Function to swap the contents of two indicated columns in a spreadsheet
swapCols(int col1, int col2){
	char* temp = NULL;
	for(int i = 0; i < nRows; i++){
		strcpy(temp, spreadsheet[i][col1]);
		strcpy(spreadsheet[i][col1], spreadsheet[i][col2]);
		strcpy(spreadsheet[i][col2], temp);
	}
}

Date: Wed Nov  8 12:35:21 2017
To: help2002@csse.uwa.edu.au
Received: from 130.95.1.70
Subject: Time Quantum
From: chris.mcdonald@uwa.edu.au

ANONYMOUS wrote:

> Am I correct in saying that a process won't be "reset" when the timeout occurs (like I've assumed 
> above) and it's moved from running to ready? So therefore it'll just take longer to execute as it?

The process certainly doesn't start from 0 (from main) again.
The Running process's state needs be saved, so that when it again advances to the head of the Ready queue, it 
may be resumed exactly from where it was suspended (with 3msec, 1msec, .... to go)
Another thread is discussing what this state may actually be/require.

Date: Wed Nov  8 12:52:06 2017
To: help2002@csse.uwa.edu.au
Received: from 106.68.153.41
Subject: Virtual Memory, Logical Addresses and Multiprogramming
From: poster071@student.uwa.edu.au
X-smilie: none
X-img: none
X-anonymous: yes


Virtual memory enables multi-programming as it enables multiple processes to "exist" 
on RAM. Additionally, a process's memory can be loaded on demand and does not need to 
exist entirely on physical memory.

Virtual memory enables processes to demand more memory then actually allocated to it 
through the use of pages which are loaded on demand when required (occurs through the 
so called "page-fault").

Furthermore virtual memory stores the processes memory in non-contiguous blocks in 
RAM. These blocks are accessed through translation of the logical to physical address. 
Only the O.S knows the true physical address of different processes' memory. This 
protects processes from accessing each others memory. 

I know it's such a vague question but is the correct line of thought? 

I'm trying piece all the topics together so I get one clear line of thought when 
talking about O.S's and hardware.

Date: Wed Nov  8 13:53:44 2017

From: poster096@student.uwa.edu.au
X-smilie: none
X-img: none
X-anonymous: yes

Yep as Chris said, try to think of a matrix of pointers just as you would a matrix of integers. 

So instead of using strcpy, just write, in the for loop:

temp = spreadsheet[i][col1];

spreadsheet[i][col1] = spreadsheet[i][col2];

spreadsheet[i][col2] = temp;

What you are doing is:

strcpy(temp, spreadsheet[i][col1]); //the contiguous bytes of memory that temp points to will 
now hold the values of the string at (i, col1). But temp is a null pointer so you will get a seg 
fault.

strcpy(spreadsheet[i][col1], spreadsheet[i][col2]); // same as above. If the string is less than 
the second you get undefined behaviour.

Date: Wed Nov  8 22:26:10 2017
To: help2002@csse.uwa.edu.au
Received: from 58.110.172.157
Subject: 2016 Exam Q2 (a)
From: poster096@student.uwa.edu.au
X-smilie: none
X-img: none

ANONYMOUS wrote:
> Here's what I've got now. I believe that I'm correctly extracting each sentence, but maybe going 
wrong
> after that...It compiles, but when I test it, gives a segmentation fault.

A few things:

First of all, like Chris said, don't increment sentences. Sentences points to the first element in the 
array and each time you increment it you lose reference to that first element. 

2. Do the reallocation at the start of the while loop, like so:

sentences = realloc(sentences, (numSentences + 1) * sizeof(char*));

The (numSentences + 1) is key because you make sure that when you add another sentence, you've 
allocated space for that sentence. You were getting a segmentation fault because the first thing you 
did was this:

*sentences = line;

If you think about it, when you get to that line on the second iteration, the last reallocation you 
did was for one pointer, not two. So your logic was flawed and hence the strange behaviour. 

> 
> 
> 
> char **makeSentences(char *chapter, int *nSentences){
> 	char *ptr = chapter;
> 	int numSentences = 0;
> 	char **sentences = malloc(sizeof(char*));
> 	while(*ptr != '\0'){
> 
> 		//EXTRACT A SENTENCE:
> 
> 		int sentenceLength = 0;
> 		char *line = malloc(sizeof(char));

3. The point of manual allocation is so the memory exists after the block of code. Here you are 
realloc'ing space for each letter, when you can just use an array, and then duplicate that array. I've 
posted full code for this at the bottom so check that out. Basically I've just gone 
'thisline[ BUFSIZ ]' and then copied the contents into thisline. 


> 		while(*ptr != '.'){
> 			*line = *ptr;
> 			sentenceLength++;
> 			line = (char*)realloc(line, sentenceLength*sizeof(char));
> 			line++;
> 			ptr++;
> 		}

4. You don't increment past the full stop after you break out of this loop. This means that you will 
not enter the loop on the next iteration and will go on like this ad infinitum. 
So add a ptr++;

Also, make sure to append the null-byte to each sentence. 
> 
> 		//ADD THE SENTENCE TO AN ARRAY OF SENTENCES
> 
> 		*sentences = line;
> 		numSentences++;
> 		sentences = realloc(sentences, numSentences * sizeof(char*));
> 		sentences++;
> 		
> 	}
> 
> 	//RETURN THE ARRAY OF SENTENCES
> 
> 	*nSentences = numSentences;
> 	return sentences;
> }

Here's what the code looks like now. It compiles and runs smoothly.

char **makeSentences(char *chapter, int *nSentences)
{
//	CREATE A POINTER TO TRAVERSE THE CHAPTER STRING
	char *ptr = chapter;
	int numSentences = 0;
	
//	INITIALISE THE ARRAY OF SENTENCES
	char **sentences = malloc(sizeof(char*));

	while(*ptr != '\0')
	{
		sentences = realloc(sentences, (numSentences + 1)  * sizeof(char*));	
		int sentenceLength = 0;

//		ASSUME A SENTENCE IS NO LONGER THAN 1024 CHARACTERS
		char thisline[ BUFSIZ ];
			
		while(*ptr != '.') 
		{
			thisline[ sentenceLength ] = *ptr;
			sentenceLength++;
			ptr++;
		}
						
//		BE SURE TO APPEND THE FULL STOP TO THISLINE THEN INCREMENT PAST THE FULL STOP. 
//		OTHERWISE FURTHER ATTEMPTS TO ENTER THE ABOVE WHILE LOOP WILL ALWAYS FAIL
		thisline[ sentenceLength ] = '.';
		ptr++;

//		WE ALSO NEED TO APPEND THE NULL-BYTE TO THIS LINE FOR CALLS TO STRDUP
		thisline[ sentenceLength + 1 ] = '\0';
	
//		ADD THE SENTENCE TO AN ARRAY OF SENTENCES

		sentences[ numSentences ] = strdup(thisline);
	
		numSentences++;
	}

	//RETURN THE ARRAY OF SENTENCES

	*nSentences = numSentences;
	return sentences;
}

> ANONYMOUS wrote:

> > Yep as Chris said, try to think of a matrix of pointers just as you would a matrix of integers.

> > 
> > temp = spreadsheet[i][col1];
> > 
> > spreadsheet[i][col1] = spreadsheet[i][col2];

> > spreadsheet[i][col2] = temp;
> > 
> > What you are doing is:
> > 
> > strcpy(temp, spreadsheet[i][col1]); //the contiguous bytes of memory that temp points to will 
> > now hold the values of the string at (i, col1). But temp is a null pointer so you will get a 
seg 
> > fault.
> > 
> > strcpy(spreadsheet[i][col1], spreadsheet[i][col2]); // same as above. If the string is less 
than 
> > the second you get undefined behaviour.
> 
> 
> So, in the exam, we would use a struct to do it, or would it be okay to do something like char 
*** 
> structure, or would both methods be valid?

Okay so this problem is actually a bit trickier than it looks. You've done this:

char*** return_spreadsheet(int nRows, int nCols)
{
	char ***spreadsheet = malloc(sizeof(char*)*nRows*nCols);
	return spreadsheet;
}

So you're declaring spreadsheet as a triple pointer but allocating to it one big array of char* 
(single pointers). Now this actually compiles but when you try to set any element in the 'matrix' 
it gives a segmentation fault. So if you go spreadsheet[0][2] you are accessing the 2nd element 
from row 0 but row 0 doesn't exist yet, hence the seg fault. 

What you need to do is malloc each row individually. Check out this website: 
http://www.geeksforgeeks.org/dynamically-allocate-2d-array-c/

Coming back to your question, yes I reckon you would need to return a structure. Because the last 
question wants to free all space in the spreadsheet, and that involves freeing each row and then 
freeing the spreadsheet. 

Date: Wed Nov  8 23:35:57 2017
To: help2002@csse.uwa.edu.au
Received: from 138.217.248.228
Subject: 2016 Exam Q4a
From: poster084@student.uwa.edu.au
X-smilie: none
X-img: none

Yeah managed to figure it out, there's a good explanation in the Stallings textbook
if you have a copy.

Essentially with 16 bit logical addresses, where the offset is 10 bits, this leaves
a 6 bit page number.
Page size is determined by the offset, 2^10 = 1024 = 1K.
The number of pages is hence bounded by the 6 bit page number, 2^6 = 64 pages.

A 16-bit system can hence manage a maximum of 64 pages of 1Kb in size = 64 Kb of memory.

Hope this makes sense, Plenty of diagrams floating around.

Date: Thu Nov  9 15:53:05 2017
To: help2002@csse.uwa.edu.au
Received: from 10.20.140.70
Subject: 2016 Exam Q2 (a)
From: poster096@student.uwa.edu.au
X-smilie: none
X-img: none

ANONYMOUS wrote:

> Wow, thank you so much for your help Conor!!
> 
> Just a small thing: it says that we need to "return a dynamically allocated array of dynamically allocated 
> strings", but we're using thisLine, which is a 1024-character-long array. So I guess we'd have to use a 
> pointer and realloc for extracting each sentence into thisLine?

 From man strdup:

The strdup() function allocates sufficient memory for a copy of the string s1, does the copy, and returns a 
pointer to it. 

So in this line:

sentences[ numSentences ] = strdup(thisline);

we are making a dynamically allocated copy of our local variable thisline. The beauty of it is that strdup 
only allocates space up to the null-byte, which we've appended to thisline. Indeed strdup doesn't even know 
that thisline is an array because, it just does its business until it hits the null-byte.

number 13.

Ah, this is what I ended up doing. Not sure if it's right.

#include <stdio.h>
#include <string.h>
#include <stdlib.h>


typedef struct
{
	
	char *spreadsheet[column][row];
	int column;
	int row;
}Cell

Cell *allocating_memory(Cell t)
{
	for(int i = 0 ; i < columns ; i ++)
	{
		for(int a = 0 ; a < rows; a++)
		{

			spreadsheet[i][a] = malloc(sizeof(char));
		}

	}
	return t;
}

void swap_contents(Cell t, int column1, int column2)
{
	char *copy = malloc(sizeof(char));
	for(int i = 0 ; i < t->rows ; i++)
	{

		strcpy(copy, t->spreadsheet[column1][i]);
		strcpy(t->spreadsheet[column1][i], t->spreadsheet[column2][i]);
		strcpy(t->spreadsheet[column2][i], copy);
	}
	free(copy);
}

void free_contents(Cell t)
{
	
	for(int i = 0 ; i < columns ; i ++)
	{
		for(int a = 0 ; a < rows; a++)
		{

			free(spreadsheet[i][a]);
		}

	}



}

Date: Fri Nov 10 12:08:51 2017
To: help2002@csse.uwa.edu.au
Received: from 130.95.1.70
Subject: Data Structures
From: chris.mcdonald@uwa.edu.au

X-img: none

ANONYMOUS wrote:

> Ah, this is what I ended up doing. Not sure if it's right.

Let's start with some basics, mapping the question's English description into C code.
A spreadsheet consists of a rectangular array of cells, yet your definition defines a type named a 
Cell which includes (as a structure member) a spreadsheet.  Wrong way around.

> typedef struct
> {
> 	char *spreadsheet[column][row];
> 	int column;
> 	int row;
> }Cell

We don't know, until runtime, how big each rectangular array of cells needs to be (and a single 
program may manage multiple spreadsheets, each having a different size), so we cannot define fixed 
sized arrays in your structure.  Moreover, you cannot provide the name of a variable to provide 
the size of a dimension, because we're defining a type at *compile time* and the variable will not 
have a value until *runtime*.

> void swap_contents(Cell t, int column1, int column2)
> {
> 	char *copy = malloc(sizeof(char));
> 	for(int i = 0 ; i < t->rows ; i++)
> 	{
> 
> 		strcpy(copy, t->spreadsheet[column1][i]);
> 		strcpy(t->spreadsheet[column1][i], t->spreadsheet[column2][i]);
> 		strcpy(t->spreadsheet[column2][i], copy);
> 	}
> 	free(copy);
> }

Read earlier comments in this thread, which explain why we don't, and can't, use strcpy() to swap 
columns, and only need to swap the values of pointers.

Nope. d_name is the file name not the full path. So future calls to opendir() will succeed 
only for subdirectories (because we're in the parent directory), not for subsubdirectories, 
subsubsubdirectories, and so on.

From: poster096@student.uwa.edu.au

Okay first of all, the question wants the entire spreadsheet to be a structure, not each 
cell. Like this:

typedef struct 
{
    char ***spreadsheet; // MATRIX OF STRINGS - OUR SPREADSHEET
    int nrows;
    int ncols;
} SPREADSHEET;

2. You can't create a 2-dimensional array with one call to malloc. This is because malloc 
allocates n bytes of contiguous memory and returns a pointer to the first one - by 
definition it returns a 1-dimensional array. So where you've written:

CELL **spreadsheet = malloc(rows * columns * sizeof(CELL));

malloc does return space for rows*columns CELLs, but they are in an array not a matrix. 
Malloc also doesn't initialise them to anything so they are probably NULL. 
Hence the value spreadsheet[0], which you think is a row of CELLs, is NULL. And 
spreadsheet[0][0] is trying to deference a null-pointer so that explains your seg fault. 

You need to allocate space for each row individually. Try this website:
http://www.geeksforgeeks.org/dynamically-allocate-2d-array-c/

My apologies - exams have me in pedant-mode! 

> How well do we need to know self referential data structures (i.e. Do we need to be able 
> to implement stacks/lists/queues/binary trees and all their functions like we did in the 
> lecture)? 

If you look at past exams, you will see most questions give you an overview of the functionality 
required of something like a data structure. e.g. the question might say something along the lines 
of :

"Queues are a classic data structure in computer science, used in a ...... etc. 
Implement a queue data structure _using a self-referential approach_, given the following 
requirements of a queue: 

1) a queue must do blah
2) a queue must allow for this boundary condition
3) the tutors obviously spend a lot of time reading Chris' exam papers" etc. etc.

Given this is a systems programming unit, not a data structures unit, if you are to be asked to 
implement a data structure, you will likely be given the requirements of the implementation in the 
question. 

Hope this clears things up.

Hi all, this is what I've got so far (Q2 2015), if anyone (Chris, Ryan or even any 
students) could have a look over it, that would be great! Thanks :)


//Define a C99 data structure to define an instance of the 2-dimensional spreadsheet

typedef struct{
    char*** spreadsheet;
    int nRows;
    int nCols;
} SPREADSHEET;

_____________________________________________________________________________
//Function to return a pointer to allocated memory suitable for storing and managing 
the spreadsheet


SPREADSHEET* returnSpreadsheet(int nRows, int nCols){
    return malloc(nRows * nCols * sizeof(char*));
}

__________________________________________________________________________
//Function to swap the contents of two indicated columns in a spreadsheet 


void swapCols(int col1, int col2, SPREADSHEET* sp){
    char* temp = NULL;
    for(int i = 0; i < nRows; i++){
        temp = sp -> spreadsheet[i][col1];
        sp -> spreadsheet[i][col1] = sp -> spreadsheet[i][col2];
        sp -> spreadsheet[i][col2] = temp;
    }
}
_____________________________________________________________________________


//Function to completely deallocate all memory allocated to a spreadsheet

void free_memory(SPREADSHEET* sp){
    for(int i = 0; i < sp -> nRows; i++){
        free(sp -> *spreadsheet);
    }
    free(sp);
}

Was hoping to get some input on my solution to the spreadsheet question. 

I've implemented it as a 1 dimensional array of CELL structs. Here it is:

#include <stdlib.h>
#include <stdio.h>
#include <string.h>
#include <limits.h>

#define ROWS    5
#define COLS    5

typedef struct {
    int row;
    int col;
    char *val;
} CELL;

CELL *makeSpreadSheet(int numRows, int numCols)
{
    CELL *spreadSheet;

    if ((spreadSheet = malloc((numRows * numCols) * sizeof(CELL))) == NULL) 
    {
        perror(NULL);
        exit(1);
    }

    int j = 0; 
    for (int i = 0; i < ROWS * COLS; i++)
    {
        if (i % 5 == 0) j++;
        spreadSheet[i].row = j;
        spreadSheet[i].col = i % 5 + 1; 
        spreadSheet[i].val = "string";
    }

    return spreadSheet;
}

void setVal(CELL *spreadSheet, int row, int col, char *val)
{
    for (int i = 0; i < ROWS * COLS; i++)
    {
        if (spreadSheet[i].row == row && spreadSheet[i].col == col)
        {
            spreadSheet[i].val = val;
        }
    }   
}

void swapCol(CELL *spreadSheet, int col1, int col2)
{
    int sentinel = INT_MAX;
    for (int i = 0; i < ROWS * COLS; i++)
    {
        if (spreadSheet[i].col == col1) 
        {
            spreadSheet[i].col = sentinel;
        }
        else if (spreadSheet[i].col == col2) 
        {
            spreadSheet[i].col = col1;
        }
    }
    for (int i = 0; i < ROWS * COLS; i++)
    {
        if (spreadSheet[i].col == sentinel)
        {
            spreadSheet[i].col = col2;
        }
    }
}

void printSpreadSheet(CELL *spreadSheet)
{
    printf("\n");
    for (int i = 0; i < ROWS * COLS; i++) 
    {
        if (i % ROWS == 0 && i != 0) printf("\n");
        printf("(%d,%d) %s\t", spreadSheet[i].row, spreadSheet[i].col,
spreadSheet[i].val);

    }
    printf("\n\n");
}

void deallocate(CELL *spreadSheet)
{
    free(spreadSheet);
}

int main(int argc, char **argv)
{
    CELL *spreadSheet = makeSpreadSheet(ROWS, COLS);
    setVal(spreadSheet, 2, 3, "toswap"); 
    printSpreadSheet(spreadSheet); 
    swapCol(spreadSheet, 3, 2); 
    printSpreadSheet(spreadSheet);
    deallocate(spreadSheet);
    return 0;
}

Output after swapping col 3 with col 2:

(1,1) string	(1,2) string	(1,3) string	(1,4) string	(1,5) string	
(2,1) string	(2,2) string	(2,3) toswap	(2,4) string	(2,5) string	
(3,1) string	(3,2) string	(3,3) string	(3,4) string	(3,5) string	
(4,1) string	(4,2) string	(4,3) string	(4,4) string	(4,5) string	
(5,1) string	(5,2) string	(5,3) string	(5,4) string	(5,5) string	


(1,1) string	(1,3) string	(1,2) string	(1,4) string	(1,5) string	
(2,1) string	(2,3) string	(2,2) toswap	(2,4) string	(2,5) string	
(3,1) string	(3,3) string	(3,2) string	(3,4) string	(3,5) string	
(4,1) string	(4,3) string	(4,2) string	(4,4) string	(4,5) string	
(5,1) string	(5,3) string	(5,2) string	(5,4) string	(5,5) string

Thanks for the advice Chris, I've now made some changes:


//Define a C99 data structure to define an instance of the 2-dimensional 
spreadsheet

typedef struct{
    char*** spreadsheet;
    int nRows;
    int nCols;
} SPREADSHEET;

_____________________________________________________________________________
//Function to return a pointer to allocated memory suitable for storing and 
managing the spreadsheet


SPREADSHEET returnSpreadsheet(int nRows, int nCols){
    
    SPREADSHEET sp;
   
    char **arr[nRows];
    for (int i=0; i<nRows; i++){
         *arr[i] = (char *)malloc(nCols * sizeof(char));
    }

    sp.spreadsheet = arr;
    sp.nRows = nRows;
    sp.nCols = nCols;
    return sp;
}

__________________________________________________________________________
//Function to swap the contents of two indicated columns in a spreadsheet 


void swapCols(int col1, int col2, SPREADSHEET* sp){
    char* temp = NULL;
    for(int i = 0; i < nRows; i++){
        temp = sp -> spreadsheet[i][col1];
        sp -> spreadsheet[i][col1] = sp -> spreadsheet[i][col2];
        sp -> spreadsheet[i][col2] = temp;
    }
}
_____________________________________________________________________________


//Function to completely deallocate all memory allocated to a spreadsheet

void free_memory(SPREADSHEET* sp){
    for(int i = 0; i < sp -> nRows; i++){
        free(sp -> *spreadsheet);
    }
    free(sp);
}

Date: Sun Nov 12 14:26:28 2017
To: help2002@csse.uwa.edu.au
Received: from 144.136.102.225
Subject: When to stat() a file
From: poster077@student.uwa.edu.au
X-smilie: none
X-img: none

d_type is a BSD extension and will not 
always be part of the dp struct.

See: 
http://www.gnu.org/software/libc/manual/html_node/Directory-Entries.html

Date: Sun Nov 12 14:31:18 2017

Previously, <poster077@student.uwa.edu.au> wrote:

> always be part of the dp struct.
> 
> See: 
> http://www.gnu.org/software/libc/manual/h
> tml_node/Directory-Entries.html

Yea I'd say to always use stat() rather than the  struct dirent  fields, cos that's 
what Chris has been using. From lecture 19:

What is each "thing" found in the directory - is it a directory, is it a file...?

To answer those questions, we need to employ the POSIX function, stat(), to 
determine the attributes of the items we find in directories

Date: Sun Dec  3 09:10:29 2017
To: help2002@csse.uwa.edu.au
Received: from 106.68.232.119
Subject: An update on the unit
From: chris.mcdonald@uwa.edu.au
X-smilie: party.png
X-img: none
X-announcefor: 10d

The results and feedback for the 2nd project are now available in csmarks.

The project average for submitted projects from 226 students was 28.8/40 (26.3/40 from 173 
students in 2016).

The project contributed 15% of the unit's assessment; we'd originally announced that the 
project would be marked out of 40 (20 marks for manual code inspection, and 20 marks for 
automated testing) but automated testing of background processes proved too difficult to be 
performed accurately.  Instead, some additional automated tests of the other steps were 
added, providing 24 marks, and the total mark then capped at 40 marks; this appears to have 
provided a better outcome for all.

Unfortunately, I've referred 19 students to the Faculty's Academic Conduct Advisor, and their 
2nd project and overall marks will be withheld until the matter is resolved.

Exam marking has also been completed.  The average mark for completed exams was 34.3/60 for 
248 students (31.2/60 for 184 students in 2016).  UWA will be releasing all 2nd semester 
results on Dec 18th (if not before) and your exam mark will then be available via csmarks.  
10 students have been offered a deferred exam in late January.

To meet the Faculty's scaling policy, the unit was scaled by 7.8% overall (by 11% in 2016).  
I believe that this reflects that the marking of the projects and exam, this year, were all a 
bit more generous.

Thank you to those who responded to the 2nd project survey.  Your feedback and comments, for 
all to see, are available from http://teaching.csse.uwa.edu.au/units/CITS2002/feedback/

Thanks very much to both Trent and Ryan for helping with the unit this semester, undertaking 
their large marking bundles in good time, and stepping in when I was away from UWA.

Good luck.
Hope to see you next year in Networks and Security '3002.
